Prosperity rises as people become more capable of absorbing new ideas and filtering out old ones.&#13; Cognitive skills that amplify our ability to take in and understand information lay the groundwork for becoming a sponge. As we become more spongelike, we become better equipped to achieve greater things. To paraphrase a verse from Hamilton, you get a lot farther by being a self-starter. That‚Äôs what Mellody Hobson did.&#13; A Hero and a Scholar&#13; In second grade, Mellody learned to read. That year she won a short-story-writing contest, and the prize was a copy of Charlotte‚Äôs Web. Although her first chapter book was a big leap, she was determined to read it cover to cover and learn the meaning of the words she didn‚Äôt know.&#13; Mellody‚Äôs desire to soak up the words and world around her only grew with age. That desire eventually landed her a summer internship with John Rogers, who had founded one of the country‚Äôs largest minority-owned investment firms. John used to read the newspaper at McDonald‚Äôs on Saturday mornings, and Mellody would meet him there even when she‚Äôd already eaten breakfast. This was how she started studying the stock market. ‚ÄúOnce she got the investment bug, she knew as much about Warren Buffett as I did,‚Äù John tells me. ‚ÄúShe was committed to learning all about this world that she was interested in. She was a sponge.‚Äù&#13; Sponginess was the first thing that stood out to me about Mellody too. I first met her a decade ago, when I was invited to give a presentation on my research to a group of VIPs. When I walked into the room, I recognized multiple Oscar-winning filmmakers and tech billionaires. It was Mellody who asked the most questions‚Äîand she was the only one to take notes. When it came to seeking and soaking up information, she was in a league of her own. Her engagement was more than curiosity: she had unusual levels of what social scientists call absorptive capacity.&#13; Absorptive capacity is the ability to recognize, value, assimilate, and apply new information. It hinges on two key habits. The first is how you acquire information: Do you react to what enters your field of vision, or are you proactive in seeking new knowledge, skills, and perspectives? The second is the goal you‚Äôre pursuing when you filter information: Do you focus on feeding your ego or fueling your growth?&#13; Being reactive and ego driven is a surefire way to short-circuit learning. It traps people in a protective bubble. They limit their access to new information and reject any input that threatens their image. Their thin skin leaves them with thick skulls.&#13; When people are proactive and ego driven, it opens the door to more information. Rather than being passive consumers, they‚Äôre active seekers of feedback‚Äîbut if it‚Äôs negative, it gets weeded out. It‚Äôs too uncomfortable. They‚Äôre impervious to constructive criticism. They become like Teflon: nothing sticks.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Filtering Goal&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Ego&#13; &#13; &#13; Growth&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Absorbing Approach&#13; &#13; &#13; Reactive&#13; &#13; &#13; Rubber&#13; &#13; &#13; Clay&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Proactive&#13; &#13; &#13; Teflon&#13; &#13; &#13; Sponge&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Learning is more likely when people are reactive and growth oriented. Responding with an eye toward improvement makes people moldable, like clay. They‚Äôre often praised as coachable or teachable. They‚Äôre not worried about whether criticism will hurt their egos; they embrace the discomfort and internalize whatever input might help their development. The problem is that they don‚Äôt seek information beyond what‚Äôs easily available. They don‚Äôt make much progress until someone picks them up and shapes them. Their growth depends on guidance from others‚Äîthey rarely take their learning into their own hands.&#13; The sweet spot is when people are proactive and growth oriented. That‚Äôs when they become sponges. They consistently take the initiative to expand themselves and adapt. That character skill is especially valuable when the deck is stacked against you‚Äîas a pair of young athletes in Africa learned.&#13; Rebel without a Coach&#13; Growing up in a rural Kenyan village without electricity or cars, Julius Yego enjoyed competing with his older brother to see who could throw a stick farther. By the time he reached high school, he had set his sights on becoming a great javelin thrower. But he didn‚Äôt have access to the right facilities, the ideal training routines, or the key equipment. Julius didn‚Äôt even have a coach‚Äîhe practiced on his own and did his best to teach himself. He was at a major disadvantage against his biggest rival.&#13; Ihab Abdelrahman was raised in a poor village in Egypt by a family that wasn‚Äôt into sports. Until he was seventeen he had only played soccer. When a teacher encouraged him to try the javelin, he won the first competition he entered. Just two years later he made the world junior championships and won a silver medal.&#13; On paper, Julius and Ihab had plenty in common. Both hailed from African communities where opportunities were limited, and sport was a ticket to a better life. Both loved soccer before shifting to track and field and discovering the javelin. But physically, it was David against Goliath.&#13; Throwing the javelin is a feat of strength. Early on, a top coach called Ihab‚Äôs arm ‚Äúone of the best I‚Äôve ever seen. He is big and strong and has a natural gift for throwing.‚Äù Ihab was built for the sport, towering at 6‚Äô4‚Äù and weighing 212 pounds. Julius didn‚Äôt have those physical advantages. He stood just 5‚Äô9‚Äù and weighed only 187 pounds‚Äîhe wasn‚Äôt a freak of nature. If he wanted to defeat Ihab, he would have to become a freak of nurture.&#13; In 2010, after seven years of training, Julius finally got to face off against Ihab at the African championships. He managed to win a bronze medal. But Ihab claimed the gold, and even his worst throw sailed farther than Julius‚Äôs best.&#13; The following year at the All-Africa Games, Julius bent down to pick up a metal spear. He stood up, cocked his arm back, and sprinted to the line. As he cranked his arm forward and launched his javelin into the air, he stumbled.&#13; Julius caught his balance in time to watch his javelin sail nearly the length of a football field. It wasn‚Äôt just a personal best‚Äîit was a new national record. Ihab finished fifth, and Julius won the gold. This time, his worst throw was good enough to beat Ihab‚Äôs best. David had toppled Goliath.&#13; Look at his trajectory compared with Ihab‚Äôs‚Äîand with the other top finishers from the 2008 world junior championships who kept competing annually in the javelin. Over time, their progress slowed, plateaued, or even reversed. During the same period, Julius did more than just attain steady gains‚Äîhe actually increased his rate of improvement.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Ihab was coachable. After receiving a scholarship to train in Finland‚Äîwidely regarded as the javelin capital of the world‚Äîhe worked with a top coach there. He listened to advice and improved his technique and his speed. But he was more like clay than a sponge. His approach was reactive, not proactive. When he couldn‚Äôt get funds from the Egyptian federation for another trip to Finland, he didn‚Äôt take the initiative to teach himself. He quit training altogether and didn‚Äôt resume until five months later.&#13; By contrast, Julius took charge of his own growth. When people asked who his coach was, he said: ‚ÄúYouTube.‚Äù&#13; In 2009, Julius went to an internet caf√© and started watching videos of top javelin throwers on YouTube. By scrutinizing their skills, he was able to start coaching himself. ‚ÄúEverything began to change in my training,‚Äù Julius says. ‚ÄúJavelin requires technique, power, flexibility, and speed and there were many of these aspects which I‚Äôd never looked at.‚Äù&#13; In 2015, Julius won the world javelin championship. At 92.72 meters, his was the farthest throw in fourteen years‚Äîonly two humans had ever hurled a javelin farther.&#13; Other than a few months of training in Finland, Julius was self-taught. He had actively sought the information he needed to grow. His form was still unconventional. As he released his javelin, he was often the only athlete to fall face-first with his legs up in the air‚Äîlike he was about to do the break-dancing move known as the Worm. He had absorbed what he saw, filtered out what didn‚Äôt apply to his unique style, and adapted his release to become the best in the world.&#13; Julius arrived in Rio in 2016 with high hopes for an Olympic victory. Unfortunately, he sustained a groin injury and only managed to complete one of the six throws during the finals. At 88.24 meters, it was still good enough to land him an Olympic silver medal.&#13; A do-it-yourself approach can be effective for certain kinds of learning. If you‚Äôre doing a relatively mechanical task like throwing the javelin, you can make great strides by absorbing objective techniques. But in many walks of life, becoming a sponge depends on filtering more subjective guidance from others. As I learned early in my career, that feedback may not even arrive at all‚Äîand gathering it is not as simple as it seems.&#13; Getting the Cold Hard Truth&#13; The message from my body came through loud and clear: You do not belong here. Between the sweat drenching my shirt and the butterflies in my stomach, I had no business being onstage. As a shy introvert, just raising my hand to speak in class was enough to give me jitters. In the days before caller ID, I even got nervous answering the phone.&#13; As a graduate student, I was determined to get over my fear of public speaking the fast way. I didn‚Äôt have time to dip my toe in the shallow end of exposure therapy; I dove right into the deep end of flooding. I volunteered to give a series of guest lectures in my friends‚Äô undergraduate classes. I needed their input to learn. But when I asked those friends for feedback afterward, they came back with vague compliments. Interesting content. Enthusiastic delivery.&#13; When they have helpful input, people are often reluctant to share it. We even hesitate to tell friends they have food in their teeth. We‚Äôre confusing politeness with kindness. Being polite is withholding feedback to make someone feel good today. Being kind is being candid about how they can get better tomorrow. It‚Äôs possible to be direct in what you say while being thoughtful about how you deliver it. I don‚Äôt want to embarrass you, but I realized it would be a lot more embarrassing if no one told you about the broccoli sprouting from your gums.&#13; When you kick off a presentation, you know you‚Äôre in trouble if you say, ‚ÄúGood morning!‚Äù and multiple people respond, ‚ÄúGreat point!‚Äù To help students overcome their hesitations, I gave out anonymous feedback forms. I wanted to become a sponge: I would absorb everything I could from the audience and then filter out what wasn‚Äôt useful. Little did I know I was going about it the wrong way.&#13; The students panned my performance. Your nervous breathing sounds like Darth Vader. Then I interviewed for my first job at a top university, and the hiring committee rejected me. No one told me why until months later, when a colleague finally came clean. You lack the confidence to command the respect of our students. The following year, when I delivered my first session for U.S. Air Force leaders, the colonels crucified me. I gained nothing from this session, but I trust the instructor gained useful insight. It was a crash course in demoralization by useless criticism.&#13; It‚Äôs easy for people to be critics or cheerleaders. It‚Äôs harder to get them to be coaches. A critic sees your weaknesses and attacks your worst self. A cheerleader sees your strengths and celebrates your best self. A coach sees your potential and helps you become a better version of yourself.&#13; If I wanted to master the art of public speaking, I needed a better filter. I decided to turn my critics and cheerleaders into coaches. I‚Äôd tried to do that in the past by asking for feedback. But research suggests that‚Äôs a mistake.&#13; Instead of seeking feedback, you‚Äôre better off asking for advice. Feedback tends to focus on how well you did last time. Advice shifts attention to how you can do better next time. In experiments, that simple shift is enough to elicit more specific suggestions and more constructive input.[*] Rather than dwelling on what you did wrong, advice guides you toward what you can do right.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; I replaced my usual feedback questions with a basic request for advice.[*] What‚Äôs the one thing I can do better? Suddenly people started giving me useful tips. Don‚Äôt lead with a joke unless you‚Äôre confident it will land. The audience wasn‚Äôt always ready for my dry humor, and hearing crickets amplified my anxiety. Open with a personal story‚Äîit humanizes you. I was trying to make it about the audience, not myself, but I was distancing myself from them instead of connecting with them.&#13; After a decade of practice, I got an invitation to give a TED talk. I opened with a story about the time I failed to invest in Warby Parker, and managed to wait a full 42 seconds to unveil my first joke, which got laughs. A later joke did bomb, and you can hear my nerves at several points, but all things considered, the talk went over well. Over the next five years, I was invited to stand in the red circle three more times, and Darth Vader only made a cameo.&#13; After every talk I give, I ask the hosts what I can do better. It reminds me that not all advice is created equal, and the more suggestions you collect, the more important filtering becomes. How do you know which sources to trust?&#13; May the Source Be with You&#13; When Mellody Hobson sent off her college applications, she was thrilled to get into both Harvard and Princeton. In an attempt to lure her, Princeton invited her to a breakfast with high-powered alumni. She was seated next to Bill Bradley, the NBA star turned U.S. senator. As she bombarded him with questions, he was taken with her curiosity and started mentoring her.&#13; One day at lunch, Bill gave Mellody some tough feedback. He told her that in his basketball days, he had seen talented players take all the shots themselves instead of passing the ball to others. She had a tendency to dominate the room, and if she wasn‚Äôt careful, she would become a ball hog. Mellody felt tears welling up.&#13; There‚Äôs nothing wrong with taking criticism personally. Taking it personally shows you‚Äôre taking it seriously. Getting upset isn‚Äôt a mark of weakness or even defensiveness‚Äîas long as your ego doesn‚Äôt stand in the way of your learning.&#13; A key to being a sponge is determining what information to absorb versus what to filter out. It‚Äôs a question of which coaches to trust. I like to break trustworthiness down into three components: care, credibility, and familiarity.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; If they don‚Äôt care about you, they haven‚Äôt earned the right for you to care about their reactions. If they‚Äôre not qualified to judge the task or close enough to know your potential, you can discount their views and prove them wrong. But if they‚Äôve demonstrated that you matter to them and they know the domain and your skills, they‚Äôre offering information to improve yourself. That doesn‚Äôt mean you have to accept every bit of constructive criticism they give. You also don‚Äôt have to agree with it to learn from it. By trying to understand what provoked their reactions, you can glean insights about how to elicit a different response next time.&#13; As Mellody processed Bill Bradley‚Äôs warning not to be a ball hog, she remembered that no one is entitled to receive feedback or advice. She thought to herself, If I cry, he won‚Äôt ever give me feedback again. She realized he was taking the time to give her tough love because he believed in her potential and cared about helping her grow. There was also no question about Bill‚Äôs credibility‚Äîfrom his basketball past and his political present, he knew what a ball hog looked like. And he had plenty of familiarity: their mentoring relationship gave him a clear understanding of her skills and shortcomings.&#13; Instead of letting the tears flow, Mellody sought advice about how she could improve. She discovered that being a ball hog was not so much a weakness as a strength overused‚Äîor misused. Her absorptive capacity could suck the air out of a room, and her quest to learn could inadvertently silence other voices. She learned that she had to adapt how she expressed her thirst for information.&#13; Mellody‚Äôs constructive response motivated Bill to keep coaching her, and the connection blossomed. Years later, he introduced her to the Starbucks founder, who invited her to join their board. When she got married, Bill walked her down the aisle, and Mellody called him the father she never had.&#13; Many people fail to benefit from constructive criticism because they overreact and under-correct. Mellody made a resolution to do the opposite: she told herself that champions adapt. She would go out of her way to show an interest in others and use her absorptive capacity to ask them questions and learn more about them. Just as her coaches had done for her, she would help people grow by dishing out tough love. That became a signature strength. Mellody gained a reputation as the board member who challenged everyone else to think more broadly and deeply, and as the mentor who never hesitated to tell the truth. I‚Äôve seen it firsthand.&#13; Recently, Mellody happened to be in the audience when I gave a new talk. Afterward, as her peers gave generic praise, I knew I could count on her for coaching. Sure enough, when I reached out, she called me with notes. My big takeaway was that I needed a clearer throughline so the attendees didn‚Äôt lose the message. She wasn‚Äôt just there to absorb and apply information for herself‚Äîshe wanted to make sure the audience could absorb and apply it too. That speaks to my favorite property of a sponge.&#13; &#13; ‚Äî&#13; At some point in the past, sea sponges branched off on their own evolutionary tree. We‚Äôre not their descendants. But that hasn‚Äôt stopped them from being good ancestors.&#13; As I read far and wide about sea sponges, I was delighted to discover something even more remarkable than their capacity to absorb. It‚Äôs their capacity to create. Sea sponges don‚Äôt just expunge toxins. They also produce biochemicals that protect and promote life with anticancer, antibacterial, antiviral, and anti-inflammatory properties. Substances from a Caribbean sea sponge spurred breakthroughs in the treatment of HIV, herpes, and leukemia. Compounds from a Japanese sea sponge have been developed into a chemotherapy drug that has extended the lives of women with advanced breast cancer by blocking cell division. And a peptide from an Antarctic sea sponge offers a promising lead in the treatment of malaria.&#13; Despite these recent developments, the greatest life-giving impact of sea sponges may have taken place about half a billion years ago. For decades, scientists believed that new animal species emerged as oxygen arose in the oceans. The latest evidence suggests that sea sponges actually contributed to this process. By filtering organic matter out of the water, they helped to oxygenate the oceans, which enabled animals to evolve. This means sponges may be partly responsible for all complex life as we know it. If not for sponges, the human race might not exist.&#13; Being a sponge is not only a proactive skill‚Äîit‚Äôs a prosocial skill. Done right, it‚Äôs not just about soaking up nutrients that help us grow. It‚Äôs also about releasing nutrients to help others grow.&#13; &#13;

 &#13; Chapter 3&#13; The Imperfectionists&#13; Finding the Sweet Spot between Flawed and Flawless&#13; &#13; There is a crack, a crack in everything&#13; That‚Äôs how the light gets in&#13; ‚ÄîLeonard Cohen&#13; &#13; When he got word of the earthquake rocking his home in Japan, Tadao Ando was halfway across the world. With a heavy heart he dashed to the airport and boarded the first flight back from Europe. As he took a boat to the city of Kobe, he felt every second pass by. It wasn‚Äôt concern for his own house that was weighing on his mind. He was anxious to check on whether the community was safe. They had entrusted him to design a number of their buildings, from a housing complex on a steep hill to a Buddhist temple.&#13; Ando had good reason to be nervous. He didn‚Äôt have any formal training as an architect. Born in poverty in the early 1940s, he was raised by his grandmother in a nagaya‚Äîa small, one-story row house made of wood. As broken windows left them shivering through icy winters, Ando escaped to the small carpentry shop across the street. He spent countless afternoons and evenings building wooden boats, blowing glass, and working with metal. He dreamed of creating his own buildings.&#13; Without the means to go to college, Ando decided to learn architecture on his own. While doing odd jobs to pay the rent, he scrutinized the structures around him. He borrowed architecture books from friends and read about the evolution of materials, techniques, and styles. He honed his drawing skills by tracing directly over sketches of buildings until the pages were black.&#13; Eventually, Ando had taught himself enough to earn an architecture license. By the time the earthquake hit Kobe in 1995, he had designed dozens of buildings there‚Äîon an active fault line. Tragically, when the aftershocks subsided, more than 6,000 people had lost their lives. Entire neighborhoods were destroyed and over 200,000 buildings were in shambles.&#13; Arriving heartbroken, Ando somberly surveyed the devastation. He ventured over roads that had split in half and maneuvered around power lines that were still in flames. He climbed through the rubble of ten-story buildings that had crumbled to the ground. Remarkably, not a single one of Ando‚Äôs 35 buildings had collapsed. As he inspected them, he couldn‚Äôt find even a visible crack.&#13; Tadao Ando is the only architect ever to win all four of the field‚Äôs most prestigious prizes. Known as the master of light and concrete, he‚Äôs revered for pioneering minimalist, sturdy structures‚Äîfrom homes to temples to museums‚Äîthat amplify the natural world around them. His buildings have been described as earthquake-proof, and his designs have been called visual haikus.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; When I considered the traits of great architects, the first quality that came to mind was perfectionism. It takes painstaking attention to detail to create an aesthetic masterpiece‚Äîlet alone a structure that can withstand an earthquake. If you‚Äôre not fastidious about getting every element right, your designs will be flawed and your buildings could collapse. But then I learned that to be uncompromising, architects have to make compromises. And I kept hearing that no one did this better than Tadao Ando.&#13; Ando is esteemed for his ability to make the most of limited spaces with limited budgets. He‚Äôs only able to do this because he fully rejects the notion of perfectionism. He knows that to be disciplined in some areas, we have to let others go. One of his specialties is being disciplined in deciding when to push for the best and when to settle for good enough. For him, this often means prioritizing durability and design while compromising on comfort. His signature style is meticulous on form but less exacting on function.&#13; When Ando designed his second house, the entire lot was less than 200 square feet. The constraints of the space meant that even if he sought perfection, it would be unattainable. He had to settle for a design with some fundamental flaws. He built a tiny concrete box without any windows‚Äîthere was only a skylight at the top. ‚ÄúAfter satisfying the minimum conditions of ventilation, lighting, and exposure to sunlight,‚Äù Ando reflected, ‚ÄúI thought the question of functionality could be left to the inhabitant.‚Äù&#13; To get from one bedroom to the other, you had to walk through a courtyard without a ceiling and brave the elements. On rainy days, you had to carry an umbrella in your own house. When the project was submitted for an architecture prize, a judge wrote, ‚ÄúThe award should be given to the brave owner who is living and surviving in this environment.‚Äù&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Ando accepted those shortcomings because he was unwilling to sacrifice his vision of being an urban guerilla. He wanted to create a well-designed, durable oasis smack in the center of a city. By closing off the walls to the outside world, he was protecting the home from ugliness and uproar. By opening an interior patio to the sky above, he was connecting the home with the splendor and simplicity of nature. Despite the deficiencies, the row house won a major prize and launched his career.&#13; We usually associate aesthetic and technical prowess with a drive for flawless results. As I‚Äôve studied the habits of great designers, dancers, and divers, though, I‚Äôve come to understand that unlocking hidden potential is not about the pursuit of perfection. Tolerating flaws isn‚Äôt just something novices need to do‚Äîit‚Äôs part of becoming an expert and continuing to gain mastery. The more you grow, the better you know which flaws are acceptable.&#13; What Straight-A Students Get Wrong&#13; When I was growing up, my mom often said that no matter what grades I got in school, as long as I did my best, she‚Äôd be proud of me. Then she added, ‚ÄúBut if you didn‚Äôt get an A, I‚Äôll know you didn‚Äôt do your best.‚Äù She said it with a smile, but I took it seriously: I shouldn‚Äôt settle for anything less than perfect.&#13; It‚Äôs one thing to care about attaining success and avoiding failure. I‚Äôm guessing you wouldn‚Äôt want a heart surgeon who was content with doing an adequate job. But perfectionism takes expectations to a whole different level. I‚Äôm not talking about the line people use in job interviews. My greatest weakness is that I‚Äôm too much of a perfectionist. It‚Äôs much more extreme than that.&#13; Perfectionism is the desire to be impeccable. The goal is zero defects: no faults, no flaws, no failures. It‚Äôs my college classmate who was so enamored with his perfect SAT score that his email handle was IGot1600. It‚Äôs the students who still display their 4.0 GPAs on their resumes and LinkedIn profiles a decade after graduation. It‚Äôs the friends who appear to be living their best lives online but hide their physical and emotional scars in shame.&#13; There‚Äôs strong evidence that perfectionism has been rising for years across the United States, the United Kingdom, and Canada. Social media clearly hasn‚Äôt helped, but the spike started in the 1990s‚Äîa full generation before anyone was posting curated images on Instagram. In an increasingly competitive world, kids face growing pressure from parents to be perfect and harsh criticism when they fall short. They learn to judge their worth by the absence of inadequacies. Every flaw is a blow to their self-esteem. I‚Äôve lived it myself.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; When I won the fifth-grade quiz bowl on world explorers, I beat myself up afterward for missing one question. How could I forget that the sea route to India was discovered by da Gama, not Magellan? When I made the finals of a Mortal Kombat tournament and won a lifetime pass to a local movie theater, I didn‚Äôt celebrate. Third place is the second loser. When I set the curve on a math test, I was disappointed. Only a 98? Not good enough.&#13; Perfectionists excel at solving problems that are straightforward and familiar. In school, they ace multiple-choice tests that have a single right answer and fill-in-the-blank questions that allow them to regurgitate facts they‚Äôve committed to memory. Michelangelo‚Äôs marble architectural membering was set within a thin, blue-gray pietra serena molding. That line is still emblazoned in my brain from the weekend I spent studying for a final exam as a college freshman, and I have no idea what it means.&#13; The real world is far more ambiguous. Once you leave the predictable, controllable cocoon of academic exams, the desire to find the ‚Äúcorrect‚Äù answer can backfire. In a meta-analysis, the average correlation between perfectionism and performance at work was zero. When it came to mastering their tasks, perfectionists were no better than their peers. Sometimes they even did worse. The skills and inclinations that drive people to the top of their high school or college class may not serve them so well after they graduate.&#13; The people who go on to become masters in their fields often start out with imperfect transcripts in school. In a study of world-class sculptors, it turned out that they were mostly average students. Two thirds graduated high school with Bs and Cs. A similar pattern emerged in a comparison of America‚Äôs most influential architects with peers who had fallen short of transforming the field. The great architects had rarely been great students: they typically finished college with a B average. Their perfectionistic colleagues had gleaming grades but went on to build far fewer glistening buildings.&#13; In their quest for flawless results, research suggests that perfectionists tend to get three things wrong. One: they obsess about details that don‚Äôt matter. They‚Äôre so busy finding the right solution to tiny problems that they lack the discipline to find the right problems to solve. They can‚Äôt see the forest for the trees. Two: they avoid unfamiliar situations and difficult tasks that might lead to failure. That leaves them refining a narrow set of existing skills rather than working to develop new ones. Three: they berate themselves for making mistakes, which makes it harder to learn from them. They fail to realize that the purpose of reviewing your mistakes isn‚Äôt to shame your past self. It‚Äôs to educate your future self.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; If perfectionism were a medication, the label would alert us to common side effects. Warning: may cause stunted growth. Perfectionism traps us in a spiral of tunnel vision and error avoidance: it prevents us from seeing larger problems and limits us to mastering increasingly narrow skills.&#13; Even if you don‚Äôt consider yourself a perfectionist, you‚Äôve probably experienced those tendencies on tasks that are important to you. On the projects that matter deeply to us, we‚Äôve all felt the urge to keep revising and refining until it‚Äôs exactly right. But traveling great distances depends on recognizing that perfection is a mirage‚Äîand learning to tolerate the right imperfections.&#13; Concrete Possibilities&#13; According to legend, a young man once sought out a master to teach him the Japanese tea ceremony. The master tested him by asking him to clean up a garden. The young man removed the weeds and raked the leaves until the grounds were pristine. As he reviewed his flawless work, he decided something was missing. He walked over to a cherry tree and shook it so that some flower petals fell to the ground. By finding the beauty in imperfection, he showed he was ready to become a master.&#13; This legend traces back to the sixteenth century, when the Japanese tea ceremony underwent a seismic shift. Immaculate dishes were replaced with chipped bowls. People drank from pottery that was worn and weathered. They called this practice wabi sabi.&#13; Wabi sabi is the art of honoring the beauty in imperfection. It‚Äôs not about creating intentional imperfections. It‚Äôs about accepting that flaws are inevitable‚Äîand recognizing that they don‚Äôt stop something from becoming sublime. That‚Äôs been a dominant theme in Tadao Ando‚Äôs architecture and his life. He‚Äôs an imperfectionist: he‚Äôs selective about what he decides to do well.&#13; If you ask Ando about his school days, he‚Äôll tell you his academic performance was inadequate. He was perfectly willing to accept imperfect scores. Despite his passion for the subject, he didn‚Äôt even excel in the architecture class he took in high school. Even if he‚Äôd been able to afford the tuition, his grades weren‚Äôt good enough to get into architecture school. He became a professional boxer.&#13; Ando‚Äôs comfort with imperfection solidified further in the boxing ring. In a fight, there was no such thing as a flawless performance‚Äîhe was going to get hit. If he wanted to win, he couldn‚Äôt get stuck in the weeds, hide from his weaknesses, or avoid challenges. There was no need to beat himself up; his opponents would do plenty of beating. If he wanted to protect his face and head, he had to leave his body exposed and take some punches. ‚ÄúIn boxing, you must risk moving into danger in order to fully take advantage of your skills and eventually win the match,‚Äù Ando observes. ‚ÄúNew building projects require the same mentality. . . . Taking that extra step forward into the unknown is vital.‚Äù&#13; After two years, Ando quit boxing. He started teaching himself architecture and honed his vision. He would strive not for perfection but for ‚Äúperfectly acceptable.‚Äù He chose to build walls out of concrete‚Äîa material his peers avoided due to its aesthetic limitations. He was drawn to concrete for its toughness and ‚Äúintricate roughness.‚Äù Since his early projects lacked the budgets to cover it up, he ended up leaving it exposed.&#13; The exposed concrete became Ando‚Äôs signature look. There are visible imperfections on every wall. You can see lines from the seams and holes that are only partially filled with mortar. To make sure it didn‚Äôt distract from the beauty around it, he would carefully smooth the concrete until it felt like silk or cashmere. As one contractor put it, Ando wanted the concrete ‚Äúvibrated till it looked like butter.‚Äù The material was still imperfect to the eye, but it became acceptable once it was perfectly smooth to the touch.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; The iconic concrete walls are on vivid display in the Church of Light, one of Ando‚Äôs most famous projects. He anchored the design on what most architects would consider an obvious flaw: gaps in the concrete that leave the back wall open to the outdoors. But they serve a stunning purpose, allowing light to beam through in the shape of a cross. After the church deemed the open air too cold, Ando compromised by installing glass. He still hopes to remove it one day, joking that every time he visits, priests beg: ‚ÄúNo glass removal, please.‚Äù&#13; Wabi sabi is a character skill. It gives you the discipline to shift your attention from impossible ideals to achievable standards‚Äîand then adjust those standards over time. But finding beauty in imperfection is often easier said than done. As a recovering perfectionist, I know from experience.&#13; In Search of the Right Imperfections&#13; After I spent almost an entire summer playing video games indoors, my exasperated mom dragged me to a local swimming pool. As I looked toward the deep end, I saw a lifeguard walking down the diving board with unusual grace. He leapt into the air, folded his body into a ball, and spun in a blur. After two flips and a dive, he vanished into the water without a splash. I was in awe.&#13; I spent the rest of the summer learning a few basic dives and showed up at fall tryouts as a high school freshman. The team coach, Eric Best, would go on to train multiple Olympic medalists. Eric said he had good news and bad news. The bad news: diving required grace, flexibility, and explosive power. I walked like Frankenstein, I couldn‚Äôt touch my toes without bending my knees, and his grandmother could outjump me. Sorry, did you say there was good news?&#13; There was good news: Eric didn‚Äôt care how bad I was. He would never cut a diver who wanted to be there. And diving was a nerd sport‚Äîit mostly attracted athletes who lacked the size, speed, or strength to star in more mainstream sports. He predicted that if I put in the effort, by my senior year, I would become a state finalist in diving.&#13; That lit a fire under me. Although I was only doing beginner dives, I was determined to get them exactly right. When it was time for me to move on, I would beg, ‚ÄúOne more!‚Äù&#13; At the end of practice, Eric had to kick me out of the pool. I didn‚Äôt want to leave until I‚Äôd erased every defect.&#13; Dives are judged against an ideal of perfection. Every flaw is a possible deduction from your score. You lose points for under-rotating or over-rotating, for finishing too close to the board or too far from the board, for failing to touch your toes or point your toes, for making even a small splash. You want a perfect takeoff and a perfect pike and a perfect entry.&#13; I thought my perfectionism would be an asset, but it turned out to be a liability. I spent hours trying to eliminate tiny droplets from my entry into the water instead of working on the larger hurdle of improving my lackluster vertical leap into the air. And along with focusing on the wrong problems, I had a problem with balking.&#13; I would start my approach, walk down the board, and jump to the end. But just before takeoff, I‚Äôd stop. I had a long list of excuses. I was leaning too far forward or too far back. I was either too quick or too slow for the rhythm of the board. I was tilting slightly to the left or the right. I was Goldilocks: I expected everything to be just right.&#13; My balking was especially bad when it came time to try new dives. At one practice, I went back and forth on the board for 45 minutes without making a single attempt. As I stood there frozen, I wasn‚Äôt just wasting time‚ÄîI was freezing my progress. I was failing to learn hard dives and only making small refinements to easy ones. I needed to get over my perfectionism.[*]&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; But how do we change? Perfectionists don‚Äôt know any other way to be. Even if you‚Äôre not one, when you care about a goal, it‚Äôs hard to be disciplined in choosing what to prioritize, what to minimize, when to stop, and how to accept inescapable flaws.&#13; Extensive evidence shows that it‚Äôs having high personal standards, not pursuing perfection, that fuels growth. Many people interpret that as advice to shift from be the best to do your best. But aiming for your best is not the best alternative. Across hundreds of experiments, people who are encouraged to do their best perform worse‚Äîand learn less‚Äîthan those who are randomly assigned to goals that are specific and difficult.&#13; Do your best is the wrong cure for perfectionism. It leaves the target too ambiguous to channel effort and gauge momentum. You‚Äôre not sure what you‚Äôre aiming for or whether you‚Äôve made meaningful progress. The ideal foil for perfectionism is an objective that‚Äôs precise and challenging. It focuses your attention on the most important actions and tells you when enough is enough.&#13; Eric told me that when announcers rave about dives getting perfect 10s, they‚Äôre making a mistake. There‚Äôs no such thing as a perfect dive. Even in the Olympic judging rules, a 10 doesn‚Äôt stand for perfection‚Äîit stands for excellence. He was teaching me the art of wabi sabi.&#13; The lesson hit home: I didn‚Äôt need to be perfect. I just needed to aim for a clear, high target. Eric worked with me to set goals for each dive at the edge of my abilities. On my bread-and-butter basic dive, a front dive pike, we started off aiming for a 6.5. But on my more complicated, shaky flip with a twist, all I needed was a 5. And if I was learning a new dive, we‚Äôd settle for anything above a 0. In other words, not a failure: I‚Äôd made the dive. Every time I came out of the water, Eric gave me a score. Then he gave me a change to make and reminded me that if I wanted to get closer to right, it had to feel wrong.&#13; I stopped waiting for the perfect approach and started going on the first one that was good enough. I stopped avoiding hard dives and started pushing the limits of my abilities‚Äîwithin a few years I was doing two flips with a full twist and a dive at the end. I stopped beating myself up for past failures and focused on recent progress. Mostly. At our team banquet, the captains gave me the ‚ÄúIf Only‚Äù award. They drew a cartoon of me saying, ‚ÄúIf only I‚Äôd pointed my left pinky toe on that entry, I would‚Äôve gotten an 8.5 instead of an 8.‚Äù&#13; If I didn‚Äôt do my best, I still felt frustrated. When divers tell him they had a bad day, Eric likes to ask two questions: Did you make yourself better today? Did you make someone else better today? If the answer to either question is yes, it was a good day. His last name may be Best, but he‚Äôs all about better.&#13; I learned that even when I wasn‚Äôt satisfied with my diving, there was a version of me that could be. There‚Äôs another technique that I‚Äôve found helpful for dumping perfectionism. In psychology, it‚Äôs known as mental time travel. Yes, that‚Äôs a thing.&#13; Expectations tend to rise with accomplishment. The better you‚Äôre performing, the more you demand of yourself and the less you notice incremental gains. Appreciating progress depends on remembering how your past self would see your current achievements. If you knew five years ago what you‚Äôd accomplish now, how proud would you have been?&#13; The 14-year-old kid who could barely do a flip would have been amazed by the progress I‚Äôd made in a few years. I started watching videos of my early diving days. It melted away my shame and marked my growth.&#13; Beating yourself up doesn‚Äôt make you stronger‚Äîit leaves you bruised. Being kind to yourself isn‚Äôt about ignoring your weaknesses. It‚Äôs about giving yourself permission to learn from your disappointments. We grow by embracing our shortcomings, not by punishing them. Make it feel wrong.&#13; Perfectionists often worry that failing even once will make them a failure. But take it from eight studies: people don‚Äôt judge your competence based on one performance. It‚Äôs called the overblown implications effect. If you cook one bad dish, people rarely think you‚Äôre a terrible chef. If you leave a finger over the camera lens, they don‚Äôt conclude you‚Äôre a bad photographer. They know it‚Äôs only a snapshot from a single moment in time.&#13; It turns out that when people assess your skills, they put more weight on your peaks than on your troughs. Even if you happened to see Serena Williams repeatedly double-fault on her serve, you‚Äôd recognize her excellence if you witnessed just one of her aces. When Steve Jobs flopped with the Apple Lisa, people still deemed him a visionary for his feats with the Mac. And we judge Shakespeare‚Äôs genius by his masterpieces (think Hamlet and King Lear), forgiving his forgettable plays (I‚Äôm looking at you, Timon of Athens and The Merry Wives of Windsor). People judge your potential from your best moments, not your worst. What if you gave yourself the same grace?&#13; In diving, my performance had a clear ceiling. I never got anywhere near making the Olympic team. But I did manage to exceed my goals. Before I finished high school, I made the All-American list and qualified twice for the Junior Olympic nationals. My proudest moment, though, was when Eric told me I‚Äôd gotten further with less talent than any diver he‚Äôd coached. I realized that success is not so much how close you come to perfection as how much you overcome along the way.&#13; The Show Must Go On&#13; In sports, excellence is fairly objective. Diving has a formula for degree of difficulty and a rule book for judging execution. But in many domains, success is more subjective. One person‚Äôs beautiful mess can be another‚Äôs ugly duckling. That makes it challenging to decide which imperfections to accept.&#13; In the summer of 2002, over a thousand people filed into a Chicago theater to see the debut of a dance musical. During the first act, the audience looked so miserable and confused that the creator worried they‚Äôd leave after intermission. In a scathing review, theater critic Michael Phillips called it ‚Äústrenuous, chaotic . . . crazily uneven . . . ill-conceived.‚Äù&#13; The show was the brainchild of Twyla Tharp, best known for her choreography of ballets for Mikhail Baryshnikov‚Äîand of dances in films like Hair and Amadeus. She had dreamed up a ‚Äútwo-hour dance extravaganza‚Äù of Billy Joel songs without any dialogue. She‚Äôd raised $8.5 million to fund it, hoping to deliver a hit that would finally be profitable for the dancers themselves‚Äîa group long undervalued in the performing arts.&#13; The big Broadway premiere was just around the corner. The devastating feedback raised serious questions in Tharp‚Äôs mind about whether it was fatally flawed: ‚ÄúWhether I or anybody else could make it work was a gamble.‚Äù It wasn‚Äôt clear whether she should reimagine the plot, introduce dialogue, develop characters, or subtract songs. Whatever adjustments she made would need to be learned and rehearsed by the performers.&#13; Just a month after the disastrous debut, Tharp unveiled a revamped show for the end of their Chicago run. When the critic Michael Phillips came back, he was pleasantly surprised at the distance traveled. ‚ÄúExtensive revisions . . . have made for a clearer, more satisfying show‚Äù that was ‚Äúoften exhilarating,‚Äù he wrote. ‚ÄúTharp‚Äôs changes may give the show a better chance at Broadway success.‚Äù She hadn‚Äôt choreographed a perfect performance, but the blemishes no longer overshadowed the strengths‚Äîthanks to her quick pivot.&#13; Pivoting is a popular concept in Silicon Valley, where it‚Äôs often said that done is better than perfect. To rapidly iterate and improve, entrepreneurs and engineers are advised to build a minimum viable product. But excellence is a higher standard: for me, that means aiming for a minimum lovable product.&#13; To create a minimum lovable show, Tharp needed to pinpoint what people were hating. It was time to be a sponge, but she didn‚Äôt know which critics and comments to trust. She needed to absorb and adapt to the key reactions while filtering out the rest. Her son, Jesse, made a grid categorizing the substantive comments by theme.&#13; Research indicates that one of the best ways to gauge the value of other people‚Äôs judgments is to look for convergence between them. If one person raises a red flag, it might be idiosyncratic. If a dozen people independently have the same issue, it‚Äôs more likely to be an objective problem. You have inter-rater reliability.&#13; Tharp and her son came up with a filter to find the signal in the noise. They decided that any concern raised independently by more than two critics wasn‚Äôt a matter of taste‚Äîit was a quality control issue. ‚ÄúThe critics turned out to be enormously useful,‚Äù Tharp recalls, quipping: ‚ÄúBless their coal-black little hearts.‚Äù&#13; The grid revealed that the consistent complaints were about the first act, not the second. Tharp was trying to do too much. One audience member was so overwhelmed by the different sources of stimulation that she covered her eyes during one song and her ears in the next. It was up to Tharp to fix it, and she knew time was running out. ‚ÄúI didn‚Äôt need the perfect solution to every problem, but I did need a workable solution‚Äîa lot of them.‚Äù&#13; A Minimum Lovable Product&#13; Perfecting the show would have required Tharp to go back to the drawing board and intersperse dialogue between the songs and dances. But she was an imperfectionist‚Äîshe was aiming for excellence, not flawlessness. For minimum lovable, she just needed to simplify the story, clarify the characters, and manage expectations. She did that with a simple addition familiar to fans of musical theater: a prologue.&#13; Adding a prologue meant she had to choreograph a new opening number in a matter of days. Rather than scrambling to design a perfect arrangement from scratch, she borrowed something from her own shelf. Her production designer noticed striking similarities between some Billy Joel beats and a dance Tharp had created for another show decades earlier. It took Tharp just a few hours to teach the recycled routine to the two dozen dancers. Now they had an opening that introduced the characters and dazzled visually. The following spring, Movin‚Äô Out was nominated for ten Tony Awards, and Tharp won for best choreography. She had found wabi sabi: the audience and the critics appreciated that a beautiful dance musical could be set to an imperfect story.&#13; Identifying which imperfections to fix doesn‚Äôt have to be a last-minute scramble. These days, Twyla Tharp isn‚Äôt content to leave her fate in the hands of critics with coal-black little hearts. After she launches a new project, she invites a small group of people to review her work in progress. They help her spot and solve the problems she‚Äôs too close to see. But they‚Äôre not just coaches‚Äîthey‚Äôre judges. Their role is to gauge whether she‚Äôs on a promising track. She recommends that we all set up a committee of judges to help us with quality control. Mine is based on my diving days.&#13; After I retired from diving, I missed the clarity of knowing exactly where my performance stood on the spectrum from failure to excellence. I decided that when I finished a first draft of an article or book chapter, I would send it to a group of trusted colleagues.&#13; For every project that matters to me, I‚Äôve had judging committees for over a decade now. They‚Äôre not permanent structures; they‚Äôre temporary scaffolding. I think of them as pop-up workshops. For each project, I pull together a different mix of five to seven insiders and outsiders with complementary skills. The group bands, disbands, and morphs as relevant.&#13; My first request isn‚Äôt for feedback or advice. It‚Äôs for a score. I ask the judges to independently rate my work on a scale from 0 to 10. No one ever says 10. Then I ask how I can get closer to 10.&#13; My target score varies with my skill and the importance of the task. For a major project like this book, I set two targets: an aspirational goal (9) and an acceptable result (8). When I get 8s across the board, I know I can be satisfied with my progress. But if only I‚Äôd pointed my left pinky toe on that sentence . . .&#13; Getting a precise score isn‚Äôt just information‚Äîit‚Äôs motivation. When multiple judges come in below 7, it nudges them to start coaching me and me to be coachable. I know I can‚Äôt settle for minor tweaks‚Äîit needs a major overhaul. I want to close the gap. There‚Äôs nothing that fires me up as much as a 4.5 on a first draft. It helps me brace myself for the feedback and advice that follows. My personal favorite: ‚Äúnot a page-turner.‚Äù Then I keep revising until every judge gives at least an 8 and some give 9s. That‚Äôs minimum lovable. I‚Äôve accepted that life is like diving: if you‚Äôre ever lucky enough to get a 10, it‚Äôs not for perfection but for excellence.&#13; We have to be careful about how much weight we put on judges‚Äô scores. A great deal of research shows that perfectionists tend to define excellence on other people‚Äôs terms. This focus on creating a flawless image in the eyes of others is a risk factor for depression, anxiety, burnout, and other mental health challenges. Striving for social approval comes with a cost: across 105 studies with over 70,000 people, valuing extrinsic goals like popularity and appearance over intrinsic goals like growth and connection predicted lower well-being. Seeking validation is a bottomless pit: the craving for status is never satisfied. But if an external assessment serves as a tool for growth, it may be worth using.&#13; Ultimately, excellence is more than meeting other people‚Äôs expectations. It‚Äôs also about living up to your own standards. After all, it‚Äôs impossible to please everyone. The question is whether you‚Äôre letting down the right people. It‚Äôs better to disappoint others than to disappoint yourself.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Before releasing something into the world, it‚Äôs worth turning to one final judge: you. If this was the only work people saw of yours, would you be proud of it?&#13; Tadao Ando asks himself this question regularly. ‚ÄúWhat some other people think of my work‚Äù is ‚Äúnot my prime mover,‚Äù he says. ‚ÄúIt‚Äôs my desire to satisfy me, and to challenge myself.‚Äù&#13; After the earthquake rocked Kobe, Ando wanted to preserve the artifacts of the past and renew hope for the future. On the waterfront overlooking the mountains, he designed an art museum. The deck features a sculpture of his own: a giant green apple. ‚ÄúIn life, it‚Äôs better to be green‚Äîand the greener the better,‚Äù Ando declares. ‚ÄúThe green apple is a symbol of youth.‚Äù Ando is now in his eighties, and his youth is reflected in his ongoing desire to grow.&#13; Aspiring to stay green is a commitment to continued growth, to staying unfinished. An apple that isn‚Äôt ripe is not fully formed‚Äîit‚Äôs incomplete and imperfect. That‚Äôs what makes it beautiful.&#13; &#13;

 &#13; PART II&#13; Structures for Motivation&#13; Scaffolding to Overcome Obstacles&#13; On the path to any goal, roadblocks are inevitable. When we run into external barriers, they often take an internal toll. The daily grind starts to bore us and eventually burns us out. Stagnation leaves us discouraged. Difficult tasks lead to failure, dejection, and doubt. We begin to question whether we can bounce back, let alone move forward.&#13; Character skills aren‚Äôt always enough to travel great distances. Many new skills don‚Äôt come with a manual, and steeper hills often require a lift. That lift comes in the form of scaffolding: a temporary support structure that enables us to scale heights we couldn‚Äôt reach on our own. It helps us build the resilience to overcome obstacles that threaten to overwhelm us and limit our growth.&#13; When psychologists study resilience in experiments, they often create an overwhelming experience by showing people distressing video clips. Think about the last time you saw an intense or unsettling scene on TV or in a movie. Your favorite character gets decapitated in outer space by a lightsaber‚Äîor devoured in the Upside Down by Demobats. If you‚Äôre anything like my kids (and my wife), those scenes keep replaying in your mind, continuing to haunt you long after the end credits stop rolling. Thank you, Duffer Brothers. But psychologists have discovered that it‚Äôs possible to stop those unwanted flashbacks in their tracks with a special kind of scaffolding.&#13; At first, I assumed it would be a form of therapy. Maybe they‚Äôd show you the clip repeatedly to systematically desensitize you (exposure therapy) or help you reframe the scene as something that can‚Äôt hurt you (cognitive reappraisal). But I was wrong. The scaffolding that psychologists offer to boost resilience is a game of Tetris.&#13; Yes, Tetris.&#13; After people watch an especially upsetting film clip, over the next week they typically have six or seven disturbing flashbacks. But if they‚Äôre randomly assigned to play a few rounds of Tetris right after watching the scene, it cuts their flashbacks in half over the next week. Somehow, the act of rotating, moving, and dropping geometric blocks shields us from intrusive thoughts and aversive emotions.&#13; To be clear, playing Tetris won‚Äôt cure your addiction or heal your PTSD. A game is not a substitute for therapeutic or pharmacological interventions. But the effect has been replicated by different research teams. At first, I just found it fascinating. As I dove further into the evidence, I came to see that the Tetris effect illustrates four key features of scaffolding.&#13; One: Scaffolding generally comes from other people. It would never have occurred to me to ward off unwelcome images by playing Tetris‚Äîthe idea came from people with relevant experience and expertise. When our circumstances threaten to overpower us, instead of only looking inward, we can turn outward to mentors, teachers, coaches, role models, or peers. The scaffolding they provide looks and feels different depending on the type of challenge we‚Äôre facing, but it has the same effect: giving us a foothold or a boost.&#13; Two: Scaffolding is tailored to the obstacle in your path. When psychologists suggest Tetris, it‚Äôs because it has a specific benefit: it changes how your brain constructs mental imagery. Brain scans suggest that Tetris blocks intrusive images by activating our visual-spatial circuits‚Äîwe‚Äôre too busy processing falling shapes to attend to the threat of unnerving images. Different kinds of games, like trivia, don‚Äôt reduce the flashbacks. Tetris is effective scaffolding because it helps you bypass a particular challenge.&#13; Three: Scaffolding comes at a pivotal point in time. It doesn‚Äôt do any good to play Tetris before you watch the movie‚Äîthere‚Äôs no imagery yet to disrupt. The structure becomes useful after the disconcerting scene, and the critical period seems to be the next 24 hours. If you wait longer, the memory has already consolidated, so you need to first reactivate the memory of the scene before you turn to Tetris to block it.&#13; Four: Scaffolding is temporary. It doesn‚Äôt take a lifetime of Tetris therapy to recover from a horror movie. Playing for just ten minutes is enough to interfere with memory consolidation and curtail flashbacks. Once you‚Äôve gotten the support you need, you‚Äôre no longer dependent on it‚Äîyou can move forward without it.&#13; Since the type of scaffolding we need varies from one day to the next, we‚Äôll find support from different sources at different times for different challenges. We might look to a coach or a mentor to show us that what looks like an insurmountable block can be rotated into a stairway. We might rely on a teammate or a mentee to show us that the key missing piece is right around the corner. And we might have to work together to reach the next level when the odds are stacked against us.&#13; Too often, it feels like our mistakes pile up, while our accomplishments disappear. With the right support at the right moments, we can overcome obstacles to growth. To learn how to build these support structures, I went to people who traveled extraordinary distances in the face of extreme physical and emotional challenges. Mountain climbers, musicians, military recruits, and athletes who beat the odds changed how I see scaffolding. You don‚Äôt have to be a sports fan to appreciate their insights‚Äîthey apply to every walk of life.&#13; Scaffolding unleashes hidden potential by helping us forge paths we couldn‚Äôt otherwise see. It enables us to find motivation in the daily grind, gain momentum in the face of stagnation, and turn difficulties and doubts into sources of strength.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13;

 &#13; Chapter 4&#13; Transforming the Daily Grind&#13; Infusing Passion into Practice&#13; &#13; It is neither work nor play, purpose nor purposelessness that satisfies us. It is the dance between.&#13; ‚ÄîBernard De Koven&#13; &#13; As she put the finishing touches on her application, a teenager named Evelyn Glennie felt butterflies in her stomach. Growing up on a farm in Scotland, she had dreamed of becoming a musician. She was drawn to the rhythm of the sounds around her: the beat of the tractor, the low hum of the cows, the clanging of the blacksmiths, the rustling of the trees in the wind. After four years honing her percussion skills and several more practicing the piano, Evelyn felt ready. She applied to one of the most prestigious conservatories in the United Kingdom.&#13; The Royal Academy of Music accepted only the cream of the crop. Alumni included Elton John and Annie Lennox. When Evelyn arrived in London for her audition, she had twenty minutes to demonstrate her skills. She played the Overture to William Tell on the timpani, various pieces on the snare drum and the xylophone, and a Mozart sonata on the piano.&#13; The Academy didn‚Äôt accept her. Multiple expert panelists voiced concerns about a lack of ability. They concluded she had no hope of making it as a professional musician.&#13; Less than a decade later, Evelyn became the world‚Äôs first full-time percussion soloist.&#13; Normally, drummers aren‚Äôt the musicians crowds flock to see. They play in the background of an orchestra or band, like Ringo sitting in the shadow of John and Paul. But Evelyn was so talented that when she toured the world alone, she routinely sold out 100 concerts a year.&#13; She‚Äôs won three Grammy Awards, for Best Classical Instrumental Solo as well as Best Chamber Music Performance and Best Classical Crossover Album. She‚Äôs performed with Bj√∂rk, played on Sesame Street, and been knighted by the queen. In 2015, she was the first percussionist to win the Polar Music Prize‚Äîthe musical equivalent of a Nobel Prize‚Äîjoining the company of Elton John, Yo-Yo Ma, Paul McCartney, Joni Mitchell, Paul Simon, Bruce Springsteen, and Stevie Wonder.&#13; When the Royal Academy of Music decided that Evelyn was lacking in ability, they weren‚Äôt wrong. Technically, she didn‚Äôt have an ear for music‚Äîshe couldn‚Äôt really hear it at all. The world‚Äôs first and finest solo percussionist is profoundly deaf.&#13; Evelyn‚Äôs ears had begun failing her when she was eight. By the time she was twelve, when people spoke to her, she could barely make out a sound. An audiologist diagnosed her with degenerating nerves and said it would be impossible for her to play music. The degree of difficulty was too high and the distance to travel too far.&#13; Being profoundly deaf made mastering music unusually hard work. But Evelyn didn‚Äôt slave over scales for endless, cheerless hours. Her school‚Äôs percussion teacher, Ron Forbes, didn‚Äôt push her through a tedious practice schedule of drills. They worked together to create the scaffolding for her to enjoy the process of learning.&#13; When Evelyn first visited Ron, he asked how she would hear music. She had no choice but to adopt a different learning style. She explained that although she couldn‚Äôt hear all the different pitches with her ears, she could feel the vibrations in her arms, her stomach, her cheekbones, and her scalp. She started to think of her body as a giant ear. As Ron played the timpani, Evelyn put her hands on the wall, learning to associate different pitches with different body parts. Some of the higher notes resonated around her face and neck. The lower notes mostly reverberated in her legs and feet. She started practicing barefoot to feel the vibrations more intensely.&#13; At the start of every lesson, Evelyn relished the challenge of sensing the sounds. As she gained mastery, Ron narrowed the pitch intervals. It was like leveling up in a video game: she was making increasingly fine distinctions between notes using only her fingertips. Soon Ron was stoking her enthusiasm by giving her a whole new set of challenges to master. ‚ÄúSee this piece by Bach? Do you think you can play it on a snare drum?‚Äù Continually varying the task and raising the bar made learning a joy. ‚ÄúThere was never a distinction between fun and hard work,‚Äù she tells me. ‚ÄúI was like a sponge.‚Äù She went on to develop her own versions of Bach‚Äôs music in contemporary drum style.&#13; We‚Äôre often told that if we want to develop our skills, we need to push ourselves through long hours of monotonous practice. But the best way to unlock hidden potential isn‚Äôt to suffer through the daily grind. It‚Äôs to transform the daily grind into a source of daily joy. It‚Äôs not a coincidence that in music, the term for practice is play.&#13; Getting in Harmony&#13; If you want to become an expert in any field, it‚Äôs not enough to be a freak of nature. No one is born with the innate ability to play ‚ÄúAmazing Grace‚Äù on the bagpipes, pull a bubbling Baked Alaska out of the oven, juggle seven balls at a time, or even spell words like onomatopoeia and mayonnaise. It takes practice to master a skill.&#13; Ever since the notion of achieving mastery through 10,000 hours of practice took the world by storm, coaches, parents, and teachers have been fascinated by a particular kind of practice. Deliberate practice is the structured repetition of a task to improve performance based on clear goals and immediate feedback. How much of that you need, however, is more nuanced than the 10,000-hours idea would have us believe.&#13; Research reveals that the actual number of hours required for excellence varies dramatically by person and activity. What‚Äôs clear is that deliberate practice is particularly valuable for improving skills in predictable tasks with consistent moves‚Äîswinging a golf club, solving a Rubik‚Äôs Cube, or playing a violin.&#13; Even child prodigies have been known to dedicate long, obsessive hours to deliberate practice. Mozart‚Äôs violinist father put him through rigorous drills and a performance schedule so grueling that one biographer called it ‚Äúunconditional slavery.‚Äù But that kind of fanatical practice takes a toll. Mozart wrote letters about how drained he felt, confessing as a teenager that ‚Äúmy fingers are aching from composing so many recitatives‚Äù and in his late twenties that he was ‚Äútired . . . from so much performing.‚Äù There‚Äôs reason to believe that he succeeded in spite of his compulsive practice, not because of it.&#13; Research demonstrates that people who are obsessed with their work put in longer hours yet fail to perform any better than their peers. They‚Äôre more likely to fall victim to both physical and emotional exhaustion. The monotony of deliberate practice puts them at risk for burnout‚Äîand for boreout. Yes, boreout is an actual term in psychology. Whereas burnout is the emotional exhaustion that accumulates when you‚Äôre overloaded, boreout is the emotional deadening you feel when you‚Äôre under-stimulated. Although it takes deliberate practice to achieve greater things, we shouldn‚Äôt drill so hard that we drive the joy out of the activity and turn it into an obsessive slog.&#13; In a study of concert pianists who attained international acclaim before turning forty, few were obsessed with their craft. In their early years, most practiced the piano only an hour a day. They weren‚Äôt raised by slave drivers or drill sergeants; their parents responded to their intrinsic motivation with enthusiasm. As they became teenagers, they steadily increased their daily effort, but it didn‚Äôt become an obsession or a chore. ‚ÄúThey practiced because they were interested in what they were doing,‚Äù psychologist Lauren Sosniak explains, and ‚Äúbecause they enjoyed working with the teacher.‚Äù&#13; Elite musicians are rarely driven by obsessive compulsion. They‚Äôre usually fueled by what psychologists call harmonious passion. Harmonious passion is taking joy in a process rather than feeling pressure to achieve an outcome. You‚Äôre no longer practicing under the specter of should. I should be studying. I‚Äôm supposed to practice. You‚Äôre drawn into a web of want. I feel like studying. I‚Äôm excited to practice. That makes it easier to find flow: you slip quickly into the zone of total absorption, where the world melts away and you become one with your instrument. Instead of controlling your life, practicing enriches your life.&#13; The importance of passion isn‚Äôt unique to music. Across 127 studies with over 45,000 people, persistence was more likely to translate into performance when passion was present.[*] The question is how to build the scaffolding to bring that passion into practice. My favorite answer is called deliberate play.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Play by Play&#13; Deliberate play is a structured activity that‚Äôs designed to make skill development enjoyable. It blends elements of deliberate practice and free play. Like free play, deliberate play is fun, but it‚Äôs structured for learning and mastery along with recreation. It‚Äôs built to break complex tasks into simpler parts so you can hone a specific skill.&#13; When I asked Evelyn Glennie how she practices, she said she spends nearly all her time in deliberate play. When she gets bored, she switches instruments, gracefully bouncing back and forth between different percussion tools. ‚ÄúIf I‚Äôm trying to stay interested in a new marimba skill, I‚Äôll transfer it to a drum kit,‚Äù she tells me. Mixing it up breaks up the monotony and keeps her passion in harmony. ‚ÄúThere is absolutely no routine,‚Äù she says, laughing. ‚ÄúThat spells hostage to me.‚Äù&#13; Deliberate play often involves introducing novelty and variety into practice. That can be in the ways you learn, the tools you use, the goals you set, and the people with whom you interact. Depending on the skill you‚Äôre trying to build, deliberate play might take the form of a game, a role-play, or an improvisational exercise.&#13; When I first read the research on deliberate play, it opened my eyes to the possibility of bringing harmonious passion into any kind of skill practice. I started wondering if I could transform the grind in more traditional job training. In an experiment with healthcare professionals, my colleagues and I found that their burnout dropped after we nudged them to inject a bit of deliberate play into their most stressful tasks. An allergy nurse started introducing herself as Nurse Quick Shot, which immediately put her young patients at ease. She let them time her, and when they came back for their next visit, they would ask for Nurse Quick Shot and challenge her to beat her previous time.&#13; There‚Äôs a movement to bring deliberate play into professional development. Medical schools have started offering improvisational comedy courses to bring levity into the challenge of learning to interpret nonverbal cues. In one exercise, Foreign Movie, students watch their classmates shout out nonsense words and try to decipher their meaning by observing their gestures and facial expressions. Students report that along with being enjoyable, deliberate play makes them better doctors‚Äîand the initial evidence is encouraging. After these kinds of improv sessions were added to a communication course in pharmacy school, students performed better on patient examinations. They were better equipped to identify a patient‚Äôs chief complaint and empathize with a patient‚Äôs concerns.&#13; These benefits aren‚Äôt limited to healthcare. In some sales classes, students were invited to learn through playing the role of salesperson and customer. In one exercise, the customer would walk up holding a box, and the salesperson would ask what was in it, with the objective of keeping the conversation going for three minutes without skipping a beat. Over the next month, when they were sent to sell tickets for a professional sports team, the students who participated in this role-play exercise sold 43 percent more tickets than a control group of students who had not completed this training. They also enjoyed the course more.&#13; The scaffolding for deliberate play is often set up by a teacher or coach, but it‚Äôs possible to make real strides on your own. If you want to improve your sight reading at the piano, you could challenge yourself to see how many notes you get right on new pieces and track your progress week by week. If you‚Äôre a Scrabble player hoping to improve your anagram aptitude, you can practice drawing random sets of tiles and see how many words you can spell in a minute.&#13; Deliberate play has become especially popular in sports. Extensive evidence shows that athletes who specialize early in a single sport tend to peak quickly and then flame out. Pounding the pavement from a young age puts them at greater risk for both physical and mental health challenges. With deliberate play, it‚Äôs easier to sustain enjoyment and achieve greater things.&#13; In sports, deliberate play is typically organized around a subcomponent of a performance or match. In tennis, for example, you might hone your serving skills by challenging yourself to see how many consecutive serves you can make. Success might be defeating an opponent, outdoing yourself, or beating the clock. You‚Äôre not counting your hours; you‚Äôre tracking your improvement. Your score is not a symbol of victory; it‚Äôs a gauge of progress.&#13; In a small experiment in Brazil, sports psychologists compared deliberate play and deliberate practice as strategies for teaching basketball to young players. Some of the athletes spent over half their training time in deliberate practice. Their coaches took them through dribbling, passing, and shooting drills with regular feedback, with and without defenders.&#13; The remaining athletes spent nearly three quarters of their training time in deliberate play. To develop their skills, their coaches designed games instead of drills. Sometimes players had a teammate who was allowed to pass but not shoot. Other times they played at a disadvantage‚Äîone against two, or three against four. Several months later, the psychologists tested the basketball intelligence and creativity of both groups, measuring their ability to move to open spots on the court and make passes that eluded defenders. It was deliberate play‚Äînot deliberate practice‚Äîthat propelled significant improvement.&#13; By fueling harmonious passion, deliberate play can prevent boreout and burnout. Although it might sound similar to gamification, deliberate play is fundamentally different. Gamification is often a gimmick‚Äîan attempt to add bells and whistles to a tedious task. The aim is to offer a dopamine rush that distracts from boredom or staves off exhaustion. Sure, a leaderboard might motivate you to push through the pain, but it‚Äôs not enough to trick you into liking a routine you hate.[*] In deliberate play, you actually redesign the task itself to make it both motivating and developmental. The best example I‚Äôve seen was dreamed up by a basketball trainer.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Out of Practice&#13; As soon as I read about Brandon Payne‚Äôs philosophy of practice, I had to call him. When Brandon was growing up in the Charlotte suburbs, his dreams were dominated by basketball. As the son of a basketball coach, he learned the fundamentals early. After spending afternoons, evenings, and weekends shooting hoops on his driveway, he became a sharpshooter known for swishing free throws and draining three-pointers. But after arriving at Wingate University with aspirations to walk onto the basketball team, Brandon ran into a problem. When he saw an open spot, he couldn‚Äôt beat his opponents to it. When he faked to one side and dribbled to the other, they stayed with him. Eventually, Brandon‚Äôs game hit a wall, and he was told his basketball career was over. ‚ÄúIt crushed me,‚Äù Brandon laments. ‚ÄúThere‚Äôs no worse feeling than someone telling you you‚Äôre done when you still love to play.‚Äù&#13; Although Brandon loved to play, when it came to developing skills beyond shooting, he didn‚Äôt love to practice. To leave defenders in the dust and create his own shot, he needed to work on his quickness and agility. ‚ÄúI was limited athletically,‚Äù he admits, and ‚ÄúI didn‚Äôt do the things I needed to do.‚Äù He didn‚Äôt do the necessary sprints to build his speed, the essential stretching exercises to boost his flexibility, or the required drills to improve his footwork.&#13; Brandon transitioned to coaching. Now he had to motivate athletes to do some of the same drills he had avoided. They loathed the wheezing exhaustion of full-court sprints that burned their bodies and the repetitive humdrum of footwork exercises that bored their minds. Like Brandon, they loved to shoot. But that harmonious passion didn‚Äôt spill over into the dullest drills‚Äîit seemed to make them even duller. The joy of jump shots intensified the dread of endless dribbling.&#13; Passion for one task can lead us to neglect the less exciting ones on our plate. It‚Äôs a pattern I‚Äôve demonstrated in research with a former student, Jihae Shin. In one study of Korean salespeople, we found that the more they loved their favorite task in their job, the worse they performed at their least favorite task. We replicated that effect in an experiment, giving people the boring task of copying names and numbers from a phone book. They made more errors if we had randomly assigned them to watch fascinating YouTube videos first. The contrast between the two tasks made the data entry even more mind-numbing.&#13; Practice involves multiple skills, and it‚Äôs rare to love them all. Brandon started looking for ways to work harmonious passion into every element of practice. Although he couldn‚Äôt subtract the pain from drills, he could add pleasure to the process. Instead of trying to push players through the most punishing parts of practice, he was going to reimagine practice to pull them in. ‚ÄúI wanted to create a system to make sure no players would fall victim to what I created for myself,‚Äù Brandon reflects. He would build the scaffolding to help athletes reach their potential by harnessing their love of the game.&#13; In 2009, Brandon set up a training center for basketball players. One day, he crossed paths with a young NBA player whose weaknesses had been readily apparent to scouts. One wrote that he was ‚Äúextremely limited by his poor physical tools.‚Äù Another lamented: ‚ÄúHe doesn‚Äôt have the size, the strength, or the lateral quickness/athleticism. . . . He probably is never going to end up being a star in the league because of a lack of explosiveness.‚Äù&#13; Brandon recognized some of his own shortcomings in the player and handed him his business card. They started working together the next morning. In his first full season after training with Brandon, the player set the NBA record for most three-pointers made. A few years later, he was named the NBA‚Äôs most valuable player in back-to-back seasons. His name is Stephen Curry.&#13; Changing the Game&#13; Steph Curry is widely regarded as the best shooter in NBA history. It‚Äôs often said that what Michael Jordan did for the slam dunk, Curry has done for the three-pointer‚Äîhe‚Äôs revolutionized the sport by turning it into a marksmanship contest. The two previous record holders for the most three-pointers in a career took over 1,300 games to set their standards. Curry eclipsed them in just 789 games.&#13; Despite being the son of an NBA player, Curry didn‚Äôt get a single scholarship offer from top college basketball programs. Coming out of high school, he was massively underrated: on a five-star scale, he was branded as only a three-star recruit. The summer before his senior year, the coach at Davidson College went to see him play. ‚ÄúHe was awful. He threw the ball into the stands, he dropped passes, he dribbled off his foot, he missed shots,‚Äù the coach recalls. ‚ÄúBut never ever once during that game did he blame an official, or point a finger at a teammate. He was always cheering from the bench . . . and he never flinched. That stuck with me.‚Äù&#13; Those weren‚Äôt the first signs of Curry‚Äôs character skills.
