When he was a kid hanging around with his father‚Äôs team, one of the players noticed that Curry ‚Äúwas like a little sponge . . . soaking up information everywhere he went.‚Äù In high school, even when he was struggling, he had the determination to support his team and the discipline to keep his cool. But research suggests that the people with the most discipline actually use the least amount of it. My colleague Angela Duckworth finds that instead of relying on willpower to push through a strenuous situation, they change the situation to make it less strenuous.&#13; A clear example comes from research on the marshmallow test. It‚Äôs one of the most famous‚Äîand most misunderstood‚Äîstudies in the history of psychology. You‚Äôre probably familiar with the classic version: psychologists put a marshmallow on a plate and told four-year-olds that if they could wait a few minutes to eat it, they would get two marshmallows. Preschoolers who managed to resist the urge to gobble up the marshmallow now for a bigger fluffy treat later ended up scoring higher on the SAT as teenagers‚Äîa finding that‚Äôs been replicated recently.&#13; When I first watched the videos of the marshmallow test, I was expecting to see a subset of kids with superior willpower. What I saw instead was kids creating bits of scaffolding to remove the need for willpower. Some covered their eyes or the marshmallow. Others sat on their hands. One mushed the marshmallow into a ball and bounced it, turning it into a toy. They had improvised their own forms of deliberate play.[*] That‚Äôs what Brandon Payne did for Steph Curry.&#13; For the Love of Practice&#13; Brandon has been training Curry for over a decade now. He told me he began with a basic principle: ‚ÄúThere is no boring in our workouts.‚Äù He set up the scaffolding to make the hardest parts of practice easier‚Äîto help Curry make more progress while relying less on sheer discipline.&#13; To make practice fun while building technical skills, Brandon created a menu of deliberate play activities. In Twenty-One, you get a minute to score twenty-one points with three-pointers, jump shots, and layups (worth one). But after each shot, you have to sprint to the middle of the court and back. Getting out of breath during that game simulates the fatigue of the real game. ‚ÄúEvery drill is a game,‚Äù Brandon explains. ‚ÄúThere‚Äôs always a time to beat. There‚Äôs always a number to beat. If you beat the number and you don‚Äôt beat the time, you still lose.‚Äù&#13; The downside of competing against others is that you can win without improving. They might have a bad day, or you might benefit from a stroke of good luck. In Brandon‚Äôs form of deliberate play, the person you‚Äôre competing with is your past self, and the bar you‚Äôre raising is for your future self. You‚Äôre not aiming for perfect‚Äîyou‚Äôre shooting for better. The only way to win is to grow.&#13; I assumed it would be ideal to practice one skill until you make progress, and only then move on to the next one. But rather than repeating the same challenges over and over, Brandon mixes them up. In twenty-minute intervals, Brandon has Curry bouncing from one shooting-and-quickness challenge to another. The variety isn‚Äôt just motivating‚Äîit‚Äôs also better for learning. Hundreds of experiments show that people improve faster when they alternate between different skills. Psychologists call it interleaving, and it works in areas ranging from painting to math, especially when the skills being developed are similar or complex. Even small tweaks, like shifting between thinner and thicker paintbrushes or slightly adjusting the weight of a basketball, can make a big difference.&#13; Deliberate play is particularly valuable for transforming the grind of summer practice. When there are multiple games per week, many athletes have little trouble staying motivated. In the offseason, though, it‚Äôs easy to lose interest. After rising star Luka Doncic showed up to preseason out of shape, he started training with Brandon and dropped weight while gaining speed. ‚ÄúUnless you‚Äôre playing pickup, summers are sometimes long. The workouts can become a little monotonous if you let them,‚Äù Steph Curry told a reporter. Deliberate play ‚Äúcreates a game-like situation with pressure,‚Äù he said, which means ‚Äúyou have to stay locked in and focused.‚Äù&#13; Over a decade of training, Curry realized his hidden potential. What he lacks in size at 6‚Äô 2‚Äù and 185 pounds, he now makes up in explosiveness as well as accuracy. He gives much of the credit to the deliberate play that Brandon organizes‚Äîit brings harmonious passion into practice. And he gets more out of it because of his determination. ‚ÄúHe loves the process. That‚Äôs one of the things that ties all great athletes together,‚Äù Curry‚Äôs longtime coach Steve Kerr observes. ‚ÄúThere‚Äôs a routine . . . but it‚Äôs really enjoyed each day. There‚Äôs a passion that comes with it, and that‚Äôs what sustains it over time. When you love something like those guys do, you work at it, you get better and you just keep going.‚Äù&#13; Although it may not turn you into a professional athlete, deliberate play can amplify your motivation and accelerate your development. One day I saw a video of a YouTuber who followed Steph Curry‚Äôs training regimen for two hours a day. At the start, he made only 8 percent of his three-pointers. Over fifty days of deliberate play, he traveled a great distance, making 40 percent of those same shots.&#13; It‚Äôs clear that deliberate play can spark and sustain harmonious passion. But can that passion can be maintained over long periods of time? Evelyn Glennie thinks so‚Äîshe‚Äôs felt it for half a century. She knows what research shows: even deliberate play shouldn‚Äôt be done all day every day. She learned this lesson the hard way.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Gimme a Break&#13; I first saw Evelyn play in 2012 during the opening ceremony of the Olympics. To build to a crescendo, she was invited to lead a thousand drummers. Standing in front of an array of multi-drums, she progressed from rhythmic taps to rapid pounds, and the stadium crackled with energy. Later, when a gold medalist entered the stadium carrying the Olympic torch, Evelyn introduced the world to a new sound on an instrument she helped design, the Glennie Concert Aluphone. It looked like a set of mushroom-shaped bells, and as she struck it with four mallets, it sounded like a warmer, more uplifting version of orchestral chimes. I had no idea she had a physical disability‚Äîlet alone one that prevented her from hearing the music she made.&#13; Back when Evelyn was a teenager auditioning for the Royal Academy of Music, the experts on the panel simply didn‚Äôt believe a deaf girl could become a professional musician. She challenged them to pay attention to the caliber of the performance rather than the impairment of the person delivering it. After a second audition, the Academy didn‚Äôt just admit her‚Äîthey ended up changing the rules for the entire United Kingdom to evaluate applicants on their musical skills, not their physical abilities.&#13; As a full-time music student at the Academy, Evelyn loved to practice. She started off playing two or three hours a day, but it wasn‚Äôt long before she felt the pressure to take on more. When she saw her peers putting in longer hours, she noticed a sense of compulsion creeping into her mind. She asked herself how long she should be practicing, and wondered if she should practice more. She started waking up an hour earlier and practicing later into the evening. But the feeling of obligation sucked the playful rhythm out of percussion, and she saw her creativity and progress evaporate with it. She began to realize that there was such a thing as over-practice. To make sure music didn‚Äôt become a grind, she decided to take regular breaks.&#13; It turns out that taking breaks has at least three benefits. First, time away from practice helps to sustain harmonious passion. Research indicates that even micro-breaks of five to ten minutes are enough to reduce fatigue and raise energy. It‚Äôs not just about preventing burnout: research reveals that when we work nights and weekends, our interest and enjoyment in our tasks drop. Even just reminding you that it‚Äôs Saturday is enough to reduce your intrinsic motivation‚Äîyou realize that you could be doing something fun and relaxing instead. Yo-Yo Ma limits his practice to between three and six hours a day and strives to avoid early-morning and late-night sessions. Chopin urged his students not to practice more than two hours a day in the summer.&#13; Second, breaks unlock fresh ideas. In my own research with Jihae Shin, I‚Äôve found that taking breaks boosts creativity when you feel harmonious passion toward a task. Your interest keeps the problem active in the back of your mind, and you‚Äôre more likely to incubate new ways of framing it and unexpected ways of solving it. Lin-Manuel Miranda dreamed up his blockbuster musical Hamilton while daydreaming on vacation, sitting on a pool float with a margarita in his hand. It‚Äôs why Beethoven, Tchaikovsky, and Mahler all regularly took walks nearly as long as their workdays.&#13; Third, breaks deepen learning. In one experiment, taking a ten-minute break after learning something improved recall for students by 10 to 30 percent‚Äîand even more for stroke and Alzheimer‚Äôs patients. Once about 24 hours have passed, information starts to fade from our memories‚Äîwe fall down a forgetting curve. It‚Äôs well established that we can avoid that forgetting curve with spaced repetition‚Äîinterspersing breaks into practice. At first, you might practice once an hour, and then start taking longer breaks until you‚Äôre practicing once a day.&#13; Obsession leads us to see rest as taking a foot off the gas pedal. We don‚Äôt stop until we‚Äôve pushed ourselves to the edge of exhaustion‚Äîit‚Äôs a price to pay for excellence. Under harmonious passion, it‚Äôs easier to recognize that rest is a supply of fuel. We take regular reprieves to maintain energy and avoid burnout.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Relaxing is not a waste of time‚Äîit‚Äôs an investment in well-being. Breaks are not a distraction‚Äîthey‚Äôre a chance to reset attention and incubate ideas. Play is not a frivolous activity‚Äîit‚Äôs a source of joy and a path to mastery.&#13; If you watch Evelyn today, you‚Äôll see that she exudes the same joy practicing alone as she expresses performing in front of the entire world. But she rarely practices in more than twenty-minute intervals before taking a break. ‚ÄúSometimes I feel like I really want to pick up a pair of sticks and do something, and other times I think, ‚ÄòNo, I just want to sit here and stare at the walls.‚Äô Other times I might want to write a little something in my notebook, or read a good book.‚Äù&#13; She tells me that when she loses interest or focus, she just stops playing altogether. ‚ÄúWorthwhile practice is where progress is made. It‚Äôs about quality, not quantity. You need to feel there‚Äôs a shift‚Äîsomething is different when you walked out of the room.‚Äù&#13; Not long ago, a mother contacted Evelyn for a consultation. After going through a series of music exams, her daughter had lost interest in practicing the violin. The mother was hoping Evelyn would give her a pep talk and motivate her to keep practicing.&#13; Instead, Evelyn improvised some deliberate play. She challenged the girl to play pieces backward, to come up with ten ways not to play the violin, and to incorporate sounds from her favorite TV show and her favorite animal. The girl left the session beaming. Before, her practice time was focused on ‚Äúan outcome of being judged,‚Äù Evelyn says. Deliberate play taught her that ‚Äúthe real outcome is her enjoyment.‚Äù Without enjoyment, potential stays hidden.&#13; &#13;

 &#13; Chapter 5&#13; Getting Unstuck&#13; The Roundabout Path to Forward Progress&#13; &#13; Every limit is a beginning as well as an ending.&#13; ‚ÄîGeorge Eliot&#13; &#13; In seventh grade, people started calling him a phenom. By his sophomore year of high school, professional scouts were coming to his baseball games. As a college student, he won an Olympic bronze medal as a starting pitcher for Team USA. That same year, the Texas Rangers drafted him in the first round and offered a signing bonus over $800,000. He would start at the top of their minor-league system and make the big leagues in a year or two. R. A. Dickey was going somewhere.&#13; Then all of a sudden, he wasn‚Äôt.&#13; When RA showed up to sign his contract in 1996, a team trainer noticed his arm hanging at an unusual angle and suggested an X-ray. Unbeknownst to him, RA was missing a ligament in his right elbow. It was a critical piece of tissue for a throwing arm, and it put a clear ceiling on his potential. His fastball might never get fast enough. The Rangers slashed his signing bonus to under $80,000 and sent him to the lowest tier of their minor-league system. Strike one.&#13; This wasn‚Äôt supposed to happen. Baseball was RA‚Äôs escape from a bleak life. He grew up poor in Nashville, and by the time he was five, his mother was dragging him along to local bars and drinking until they closed. His parents divorced a few years later, and his father was largely absent. Feeling abandoned left him with something to prove.&#13; For seven years, RA toiled away in minor-league baseball. It felt as if he was wasting the prime of his career. Since he couldn‚Äôt throw a blazing fastball, he honed his ability to fool batters by varying speed and spin. Finally, in his late twenties, he got his big break: the Rangers brought him up to the majors full time.&#13; It didn‚Äôt take long for RA to realize he was out of his league. Scouts and journalists gave brutal assessments of his performance and potential. Journeyman. Marginal. Mediocre. He gave up too many runs and lost more games than he won. He knew what that meant. Has-been. Never-was.&#13; Partway through his third major-league season with the Rangers, his managers brought him in for a tough conversation. They told him he was ‚Äúgoing nowhere,‚Äù he reflects, ‚Äúan assessment that I could hardly argue with. I‚Äôd been going nowhere for a long time.‚Äù They demoted him back down to the minors. Strike two.&#13; RA was determined to return to the majors. In the offseason he threw countless pitches against cinder blocks and kept a baseball in his car to refine his grip while driving. He pushed himself harder than ever.&#13; The following season, the Rangers gave him another chance. In his first game back, he tied a major-league record.&#13; But not the good kind of record. RA gave up six home runs in just three innings‚Äîno pitcher had ever done worse. As the crowd booed, the Rangers yanked him out of the game and sent him down to the minors . . . again. Strike three. You‚Äôre out.&#13; Pitchers usually peak in their mid- to late twenties and retire by their early 30s. At 31, it was clearly too late for RA to make a comeback. The writing was on the wall he kept hitting: his baseball career was over.&#13; One of the most frustrating parts of honing a skill is getting stuck. Instead of continuing to improve, you start to stagnate. It feels as if you‚Äôve reached the upper bound of your mental or physical capacities. Since stagnation marks the end of growth, it seems to spell the beginning of decline. My best days are behind me. It‚Äôs all downhill from here. Surgeons expect to stagnate and decline as their eyesight and reflexes deteriorate. Scientists prepare to stagnate and decline as their neurons die. Athletes inevitably stagnate and decline as their strength and speed wane. Or at least that‚Äôs what we assume. But the reality is less linear‚Äîand more uplifting.&#13; At age 35, R. A. Dickey broke through his wall once and for all. After spending the bulk of fourteen years in the minor leagues, he made it back to the majors. That year, his earned run average made him one of the ten best pitchers in all of baseball, and he signed a multiyear, multimillion-dollar contract with the New York Mets. Of the nine pitchers who had outranked him in his draft class, eight had already retired, and the ninth would never make it back to the majors. Yet RA was just beginning to realize his hidden potential.&#13; The key to his eventual triumph was the scaffolding others helped him build. It came from many different sources, and it took RA a while to put the pieces together. But he never would‚Äôve gotten unstuck if his coaches hadn‚Äôt started by sending him back to the drawing board.&#13; A rut is not a sign that you‚Äôve tanked. A plateau is not a cue that you‚Äôve peaked. They‚Äôre signals that it may be time to turn around and find a new route. When you‚Äôre stuck, it‚Äôs usually because you‚Äôre heading in the wrong direction, you‚Äôre taking the wrong path, or you‚Äôre running out of fuel. Gaining momentum often involves backing up and navigating your way down a different road‚Äîeven if it‚Äôs not the one you initially intended to travel. It might be unfamiliar, winding, and bumpy. Progress rarely happens in a straight line; it typically unfolds in loops.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Backing Up to Move Forward&#13; Skills don‚Äôt grow at a steady pace. Improving them is like driving up a mountain. As we climb higher and higher, the road gets steeper and steeper, and our gains get smaller and smaller. When we run out of momentum, we start to stall. It‚Äôs not enough to step on the gas‚Äîour wheels are spinning, but we‚Äôve stopped moving.&#13; After poring over more than a century of evidence on progress, cognitive scientists Wayne Gray and John Lindstedt observed a fascinating arc. When our performance stagnates, before it improves again, it declines. When people‚Äôs skills stalled in tasks ranging from Tetris to golf to memorizing facts, they didn‚Äôt usually ascend again until after they had deteriorated.&#13; When we reach a dead end, to move forward, we may have to head back down the mountain. Once we‚Äôve retreated far enough, we can find another way‚Äîa path that will allow us to build the momentum needed to reach the peak.&#13; It‚Äôs often difficult to accept that we need to retreat. Backing up means scrapping our current plan and starting over. That‚Äôs what causes a temporary decline in performance: we‚Äôve chosen to give up the gains we‚Äôve made. We‚Äôre regressing in order to progress. ‚ÄúPerformance suffers as new methods are being invented, tested, rejected, or accepted,‚Äù Gray and Lindstedt explain. We ascend after ‚Äúthe implementation of a successful new method . . . to surpass prior levels of achievement.‚Äù&#13; Finding the right method involves trial and error. Some trials will just be plain errors: we spin our wheels on bad strategies. But even if we discover a better method, our inexperience with it will usually make us worse at first. Those backsteps aren‚Äôt only normal‚Äîin many situations they‚Äôre necessary.&#13; In typing, if you hunt and peck, you‚Äôll probably level off around 30 to 40 words per minute. No matter how hard you practice, you‚Äôll hit that wall. If you want to double your pace to 60 to 70 words a minute, you have to try a new method: typing by touch instead of sight. But before you can speed up, you have to slow down. It takes time to learn the keys by heart.&#13; More advanced skills come with steeper learning curves. If you‚Äôre solving a Rubik‚Äôs Cube, the easiest method is layer by layer. You make a blue cross on one side, then rotate it to fill in the corners and start working on the next side. About 130 moves later, you‚Äôre done. If you want to do it faster, you‚Äôll need to memorize a list of algorithms. It will take you longer at first, but eventually you‚Äôll only need 60 moves. In the process, you‚Äôll also need to rebuild your muscle memory‚Äîshedding old habits in favor of new ones.&#13; One of the surprising things about backing up is that it can set the stage for improvement even when you don‚Äôt do it intentionally. In a study of over 28,000 NBA basketball games, researchers investigated what happened to teams after their star players got injured. As expected, teams got worse. But once the star returned, they won even more games than they had before he was hurt. Why did losing their best player ultimately help them get better?&#13; Without their stars, teams had to go back to square one and search for new paths to success. They rearranged their roles to enable peripheral players to step up and drew up fresh plays to leverage their strengths. When the star came back, their shot balance improved. They were less dependent on one hero to carry the entire team.[*]&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Thicker lines represent more passes&#13; &#13; &#13; You can see a similar pattern in NHL hockey teams after a player got injured. The more teams experimented with their lineups to bring different players together on the ice, the better they performed.&#13; It shouldn‚Äôt take an extreme event like an injury to push us to stop, reverse, and switch routes. But the truth is we‚Äôre often afraid to go backward. We see slowing down as losing ground, backing up as giving up, and rerouting as veering off course. We worry that when we step back, we‚Äôll lose our footing altogether. This means we stay exactly where we are‚Äîsteady but stuck. We need to embrace the discomfort of getting lost.&#13; A Swing and a Miss&#13; Backing up puts us on new terrain‚Äîwe‚Äôre in uncharted territory. We‚Äôre taking an unfamiliar path to a destination we‚Äôve never visited, and the summit might not even be visible at the start. To find our way, we need scaffolding in the form of some basic navigational tools.&#13; The bad news is that a perfect map won‚Äôt exist. The exact route hasn‚Äôt been plotted for us‚Äîthere may not even be a road. We might have to pave our own way, figuring out the route as we go, one turn at a time.&#13; The good news is that to start moving, we don‚Äôt actually need a map. All we need is a compass to gauge whether we‚Äôre heading in the right direction.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Depending on the skill you‚Äôre trying to learn, you might discover a compass in a book, on the web, or in a conversation. A good compass signals when you‚Äôre going off course and orients you in a better direction. If you begin to stall as you learn to code in C++, you can find a compass in a quick online search that points you toward Python: it‚Äôs easier to learn and just as well suited to accomplish a wide variety of projects. If your oil paintings keep coming out lumpy, you might pick up a compass in a chat with a seasoned artist who suggests a solvent to thin your paints. And if you‚Äôre a baseball pitcher trying to climb out of what feels like a permanent slump, your compass might come from a coach who tells you your fastball is too slow‚Äîand points you toward a new pitch.&#13; That was the beginning of R. A. Dickey‚Äôs transformation. I sought him out because I‚Äôd never seen someone make such a dramatic improvement after being stalled for so long and taking so many steps backward. If anyone knew how to get unstuck, it was the guy who transitioned from one of the worst in his field to one of the best.&#13; After nearly a decade as a minor-league pitcher, RA was struggling to build a career and support his family. To pay the bills during the offseason, one spring he sold golf balls that he fished out of alligator-infested lagoons. In the span of a decade, he moved more than thirty times, only to find himself in the exact same place. It was as if he was stuck in quicksand: the harder he tried to pull himself out, the harder it became to escape.[*]&#13; When RA made his final trip down to the minors at age 31, his pitching coaches handed him a compass. They told him he was heading in the wrong direction: he was never going to make it back to the majors on his current course. In an attempt to salvage his career, they pointed him toward a murky, mysterious path that few had navigated. For years, as part of his repertoire, RA had occasionally hurled a strange pitch‚Äîhe named it the Thing. His coaches recognized the way he was holding the ball: it was similar to the grip for a rare pitch called the knuckleball. They encouraged RA to develop it and make it the centerpiece of his game.&#13; Instead of flying with vicious speed and deadly spin, a knuckleball sails slower and as flat as possible. Rather than wrapping your fingers around the ball, you dig the nails of your index and middle fingers into it. Those two knuckles stick up in the air, giving the pitch its signature name. That unusual grip takes the rotation off the ball, allowing it to zigzag erratically in the air and befuddle batters.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; The knuckleball is so unpredictable that catchers wear oversized mitts to snag it. Since it doesn‚Äôt require great strength or cause unusual strain, it can add years of longevity to a pitcher‚Äôs arm. But as difficult as it is to hit and catch, the knuckleball is even tougher to pitch‚Äîand as RA would find out, even tougher than that to master.&#13; There was no obvious way for RA to develop his skills. His pitching coaches had never worked with a knuckleballer before‚Äîthey couldn‚Äôt give him a map, because they didn‚Äôt have one. There weren‚Äôt any knuckleball textbooks or tutorials. All they could offer was a compass pointing him in the general direction of throwing a pitch that doesn‚Äôt spin.&#13; Everything about the knuckleball felt backward for RA. To keep the ball from spinning, as he released it, he was supposed to keep his wrist as stiff as possible. But from the time he was a kid, he had been taught to rotate his wrist rapidly as he let the ball fly. Fastballs have backspin, curveballs have topspin, sinkers have sidespin. ‚ÄúI had to unlearn all of that,‚Äù RA told me, and ‚Äúrelearn my mechanics. It took major deconstruction to be able to reconstruct. I had to tear it down to ground zero to rebuild.‚Äù There was no guarantee that his effort would succeed.&#13; Seeking discomfort was a character skill he fully embraced. But RA‚Äôs early outings with knuckleballs did not go smoothly. In his first minor-league game throwing the pitch, he gave up twelve runs in six innings. After the debacle of debuting his knuckleball in the majors‚Äîthe game where he tied the all-time worst record for homers given up‚Äîthe Rangers let him go from their organization altogether. Still, he thought his knuckleball had the potential to go somewhere good. He just didn‚Äôt know how to get there.&#13; The drawback of a compass is that it only gives you direction‚Äînot directions. It can help you back away from the wrong path and point you toward a better one. But to navigate that path effectively, you need a guide.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Janis Ozolins from OzolinsJanis.com&#13; &#13; &#13; What Those Who Can Do Can‚Äôt Teach&#13; When we‚Äôre unsure of the path to a goal, we often seek directions from an expert guide. It‚Äôs a familiar mantra: if you want to be great, learn from the best. Take a master class in cooking from a top chef. Sign your kids up for a tennis lesson with a pro. Convince the biggest star in your field to be your mentor and learn how to follow in their footsteps. What could be better than taking your first physics class with Einstein?&#13; Quite a bit, it turns out. In a clever study, economists wanted to find out whether students really learn more from experts. They collected data on every freshman at Northwestern University from 2001 to 2008. They investigated whether freshmen did better in their second course in a subject if their introductory class was taught by more qualified instructors. You might assume that students would be better off learning the basics from an expert (a tenure-track or tenured professor) than a nonexpert (a lecturer with less specialized knowledge). But the data showed the opposite: students who took their initial class with an expert ended up with poorer grades in the next class.&#13; The pattern was robust across fields: students learned less from introductory classes taught by experts in every subject. It held across years‚Äîwith over 15,000 students‚Äîand in courses with tougher as well as easier grading. And the experts were especially bad at teaching students who were less academically prepared.[*]&#13; It turns out that if you‚Äôre taking a new road, the best experts are often the worst guides. There are at least two reasons why experts struggle to give good directions to beginners. One is the distance they‚Äôve traveled‚Äîthey‚Äôve come too far to remember what it‚Äôs like being in your shoes. It‚Äôs called the curse of knowledge: the more you know, the harder it is for you to fathom what it‚Äôs like to not know. As cognitive scientist Sian Beilock summarizes it, ‚ÄúAs you get better and better at what you do, your ability to communicate your understanding or to help others learn that skill often gets worse and worse.‚Äù&#13; That was Einstein‚Äôs curse in the classroom. He knew too much, and his students knew too little. He had so many ideas swirling in his head that he had a hard time keeping his lectures organized‚Äîlet alone explaining to a beginner how gravity bends light. When he made his teaching debut in a thermodynamics course, despite being a rising star in physics, his lackluster teaching attracted only three students. His material was often over their heads, and after he failed to draw a larger group the following semester, Einstein canceled the class. Several years down the road, he was nearly denied another faculty position because the university president was underwhelmed by his teaching skills.&#13; It‚Äôs often said that those who can‚Äôt do, teach. It would be more accurate to say that those who can do, can‚Äôt teach the basics. A great deal of expert knowledge is tacit‚Äîit‚Äôs implicit, not explicit. The further you progress toward mastery, the less conscious awareness you often have of the fundamentals. Experiments show that skilled golfers and wine aficionados have a hard time describing their putting and tasting techniques‚Äîeven asking them to explain their approaches is enough to interfere with their performance, so they often stay on autopilot. When I first saw an elite diver do four and a half somersaults, I asked how he managed to spin so fast. His answer: ‚ÄúJust go up in a ball.‚Äù Experts often have an intuitive understanding of a route, but they struggle to articulate all the steps to take. Their brain dump is partially filled with garbage.&#13; Instead of helping you find your way, directions from expert guides can leave you stuck. Even worse, they can leave you feeling like your own limitations are preventing you from progressing. I started college torn between majoring in psychology and physics‚Äîmy two favorite subjects in high school. I was thrilled to get into a class with an eminent astrophysics professor. One day he proclaimed that the universe was everything and presented evidence that it was expanding . . . but couldn‚Äôt explain what it was expanding into. I went from feeling fascinated to frustrated and confident to incompetent. Although he was passionate and caring, he had learned too much‚Äîand been a novice too long ago‚Äîto relate to my ignorance. I never took another physics course.&#13; Even if your chosen expert can walk you through their route, when you ask for directions on yours, you‚Äôll run into a second challenge. You don‚Äôt share the same strengths and weaknesses‚Äîtheir hills and valleys aren‚Äôt the same as yours. You might be heading for the same destination, but you‚Äôre starting far from their position. This makes your path as unfamiliar to them as theirs is to you.&#13; Of course, you‚Äôll get more personalized advice from a guide who knows you well. But as tempting as it is to turn to a trusted mentor for sage advice, no individual will have all the right directions. You can see this in a study of lawyers navigating the path to partner. Guidance from a single mentor didn‚Äôt make a difference in promotions. There were other upsides: lawyers who had a supportive mentor were more satisfied and committed than their peers who lacked one. But when it came to getting promoted to partner, what mattered was being guided by multiple mentors. Different mentors were able to share different tidbits on how to advance. It didn‚Äôt take a village, though‚Äîall it took was two or three mentors to help lawyers make the climb to partner rather than seeing their careers stall.&#13; Just as it‚Äôs unwise to seek rudimentary instruction from the most eminent experts, it‚Äôs a mistake to rely on a single guide. No one else knows your exact journey. But if you collect directions from multiple guides, they can sometimes combine to reveal routes you didn‚Äôt see. The more uncertain the path and the higher the peak, the greater the range of guides you‚Äôll need. The challenge is to piece the various tips together into a route that works for you.&#13; Writing Your Own Guidebook&#13; Learning from multiple guides is an iterative, interactive process. It‚Äôs not as simple as going to people and asking, ‚ÄúCan I pick your brain?‚Äù Also, the image of picking a brain is gross. The information isn‚Äôt just sitting there, waiting to be extracted. We don‚Äôt live in the Matrix. Guides can‚Äôt simply upload their insights for us to download.&#13; The point of engaging guides isn‚Äôt to blindly follow their leads. It‚Äôs to chart possible paths to explore together. To do that, you have to make their implicit knowledge explicit. Being a sponge starts with seeking their advice‚Äîbut instead of asking to pick their brain, you ask them to retrace their route.&#13; The goal is to get your guides to drop pins‚Äîthe key landmarks and turning points from their climbs. To jog their memories of paths long forgotten, you might inquire about the crossroads they faced. Those could be skills they sought out, advice they took or ignored, or changes they made. It can also help to tell them about the roads you‚Äôve taken so far. As they learn about your prior paths and current location, they may begin to notice avenues for progress that they didn‚Äôt think to point out originally.&#13; The pins you gather won‚Äôt form an accurate map. Some won‚Äôt apply to you‚Äîone pin might lead you across a stream, and your bicycle makes for a terrible boat. Some may no longer apply at all‚Äîthey‚Äôll take you to a road that‚Äôs closed. You may end up doing plenty of loops before you find the right path. And your guides are likely to be unaware of bridges that have only recently been built.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; When I asked R. A. Dickey how he found his way, one of the first things he mentioned was the number of guides he had to enlist. He didn‚Äôt have a single ongoing mentor. When RA started his journey in 2005, Tim Wakefield was the only active knuckleballer in the majors. Few others had even tried it; there were only about a dozen retired players who had wielded the pitch successfully. There wasn‚Äôt one expert who could unlock all the mysteries of the knuckleball or one coach who could give him all the directions. He had to become a sponge in finding credible sources, filtering out the tips that weren‚Äôt relevant to him, and adapting his approach accordingly.&#13; After many months of struggling solo, RA took the initiative to seek out some guides. Since it was a long and winding road, he needed a range of perspectives. He started reaching out to the handful of people alive who had become knuckleball wizards. His hope was to collect some of their pins. They didn‚Äôt have a full set of ready-made solutions‚Äîthey surfaced ideas for RA to test.&#13; In 2008, before a game against Tim Wakefield‚Äôs team, RA convinced him to offer some guidance. That‚Äôs how lonely the knuckleball road is: a player will hand his trade secrets to an opponent just to keep the pitch alive. After watching him throw and asking questions, RA came away with a new path to try: he should make sure his arm went down the center of his body as he released the ball. The following year he made a pilgrimage to see Hall of Famer Phil Niekro, the greatest knuckleballer ever. Niekro noticed that he wasn‚Äôt pushing his hips forward as he threw, which gave him another road to test out. And RA made multiple trips to see another retired knuckleballer, Charlie Hough, who taught him to adjust his grip and streamline his windup. To keep the ball from spinning, RA learned to picture himself standing in a doorway and executing the entire throw without letting his body touch the doorframe. It restricted his arm to the point that he felt like a Tyrannosaurus rex, but it became a pivotal turn.&#13; RA also had to learn which pins to ignore. His pitching coaches kept telling him to throw the ball slower, in the 60-miles-per-hour zone Wakefield and Hough had favored. But after experimenting with different speeds, RA found that his best knuckleballs often zipped at 80 miles per hour.&#13; Sometimes we need to discover things no guide can provide and write our own directions. Through trial and error, RA discovered that he needed to learn a new skill: the art of the manicure. To throw a good knuckleball, his fingernails had to be long enough to get traction but short enough not to break. He became a one-man nail salon.&#13; After three years of loops, RA was no longer stuck. He was on a path forward, thanks to a guidebook he had written himself.&#13; Even so, that path was not an easy one. His guides had warned him that the emotional journey might be as bumpy as the physical one. Since the knuckleball didn‚Äôt fly like a bullet, it was impossible to aim‚Äîall he could do was let it go like a butterfly. He would have to embrace imperfection: his performance would be as erratic as the flight path of the pitch itself. ‚ÄúWithout talking to people who walked a mile in my shoes, I would not have been able to trust that there was a turning point in the future,‚Äù RA told me. ‚ÄúHope is incredible fuel. I had people to help me sustain that hope.‚Äù&#13; He estimates that he threw over 30,000 knuckleballs at brick walls, cinder blocks, and nets before he had a consistent command of the mechanics. The glacial pace of progress left him wondering if he would ever make it in the big leagues. It didn‚Äôt help when he tied another ‚Äúworst ever‚Äù record in 2008. Maybe he was destined to go down in history as Mr. Four Wild Pitches in One Inning. As he described it to me, ‚ÄúWhen you throw yourself into something and don‚Äôt see results, it‚Äôs discouraging.‚Äù&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Running on Empty&#13; Getting discouraged is a common obstacle after turning around. That‚Äôs because going backward doesn‚Äôt always lead directly to a new peak. Sometimes you end up stuck, and it‚Äôs not because you‚Äôre on the wrong path. It‚Äôs because your path is taking you in long circles toward the top, and you can‚Äôt even tell that you‚Äôre gaining ground. You‚Äôre not seeing enough progress to maintain your motivation.&#13; There‚Äôs a name for that feeling: it‚Äôs called languishing. Languishing is a sense of stagnation and emptiness. The term was coined by a sociologist (Corey Keyes) and immortalized by a philosopher (Mariah Carey).&#13; Languishing is the emotional experience of stalling. You may not be depressed or burned out, but you definitely feel blah. Every day starts with a case of the Mondays. You‚Äôre muddling through the moments, watching your weeks go by in shades of gray.[*]&#13; While writing this chapter, I struggled to find the right framework to capture nonlinear progress. I tried countless ideas that didn‚Äôt work‚Äîdemolishing and renovating a building, digging a tunnel, breaking through a wall, uprooting a plant. That draft got a 4/10 from my judges‚Äîand they were being generous. They came up with a mantra: kill the plant. I kept going back to the drawing board. When nothing resonated, I tried to revive the plant. They came back with a Weedwacker.&#13; After several weeks, I‚Äôd gone in so many circles that I started to languish. The irony of getting stuck on the chapter about getting unstuck was not lost on me. I was meta-stuck and not at all amused. As a linear thinker and disciplined writer, I typically start my morning at the keyboard with a clear vision. Not having one was unsettling. Staring at the blinking cursor on my blank screen, I decided to investigate the origins of the term. Is it called a cursor in homage to all the writers who‚Äôve cursed it? By dinnertime, it felt as if I‚Äôd wasted the entire day‚Äîit was exasperating. Searching for something to take my mind off the chapter, I stayed up too late eating ice cream and hanging out with my friends Monica and Chandler. Hello, revenge bedtime procrastination. It didn‚Äôt help. My tank was near empty.[*]&#13; As I‚Äôve studied hidden potential, I‚Äôve realized that languishing is more than the feeling of being stuck. It also keeps you stuck. Research shows that languishing disrupts your focus and dulls your motivation. It becomes a Catch-22: you know you need to do something, but you doubt whether it will do anything. That‚Äôs when you need to pull off the freeway and refuel.&#13; Taking a Detour&#13; When I ask people what it takes to achieve greater things, one of the most common answers is that you need to be laser focused and single-minded in your dedication. You need to double down and block out anything that threatens to drain your energy or divide your attention. If you want to excel at your job, spend more time at work: get in early, go home late. Put your hobbies on the back burner . . . and definitely don‚Äôt take on a side hustle. You don‚Äôt want to wind up distracted and exhausted.&#13; But the evidence tells a different story. A digression doesn‚Äôt have to be a diversion. It can be a source of energy.&#13; In one study, when people had spent engaging evenings on their side hustles, they performed better the next day in their regular jobs. The progress they made at night put an extra spring in their step the following morning. The motivation benefits outweighed any distraction costs.&#13; Hobbies have similar benefits. In another study, when people took on serious hobbies at home, their confidence climbed at work‚Äîbut only if the hobbies were in a different area from their jobs. If you‚Äôre an artist and you‚Äôre languishing, doing ceramics on the side won‚Äôt do much to spur a sense of mastery. But if you‚Äôre feeling meh as a social worker or an accountant, a pottery project might just be a new path to progress.&#13; Of all the factors that have been studied, the strongest known force in daily motivation is a sense of progress. You can‚Äôt always find motivation by staring harder at the thing that isn‚Äôt working. Sometimes you can build momentum by taking a detour to a new destination.&#13; A detour is a route off your main road that you take to refuel. You‚Äôre not taking a break; you‚Äôre not sitting still, idling. You‚Äôre temporarily veering off course, but you‚Äôre still in motion. You‚Äôre advancing toward a different goal.&#13; Psychologists find that achieving a sense of progress doesn‚Äôt require huge gains. Fuel can come from small wins. When you make headway, even if you‚Äôve turned off the main road, it reminds you that forward movement is possible. Instead of feeling daunted by the long road ahead, you‚Äôre ready to make the next turn.&#13; When I was spinning my wheels on this chapter, I realized I needed to take my own message to heart. My diversion of choice was online Scrabble‚Äîa longtime hobby of mine. A few games in, I had r-a-l-g-n-o-i. I unscrambled it and joined it with an open i on the board. Original. The small win was the fuel I needed. I was ready to get back on the main road to work on the chapter.&#13; My first step was to reset my expectations. I wouldn‚Äôt crack the whole chapter in one sitting. Instead of waiting for the perfect map, I should just start making one small turn at a time. Kill the plant. Find a better overarching metaphor (navigation). Pick one key tool (a compass). I‚Äôd avoided those since I have a terrible sense of direction. It‚Äôs so bad that when I make a U-turn, my in-laws call it the Adam Turn. With a few small wins, I started to gain speed. Some of the turns would take me backward, but together they‚Äôd help me move forward‚Äîmuch like R. A. Dickey did.&#13; At first, RA had found his own small wins without a detour. He tracked his progress by counting how many pitches he could throw without spin. Each pitch that didn‚Äôt spin was a jolt of motivation. Within a few years, he had improved from being able to throw a good knuckleball half the time to about three quarters of the time.&#13; By the time the Mets recruited him in 2010, at age 35, RA was a solid big-league pitcher. But he still hadn‚Äôt reached the top of his mountain. If his fast knuckleball didn‚Äôt zig or zag enough, batters would crush it. To keep moving forward, he needed something more in his tank.&#13; He decided to refuel by finding a new mountain to climb. Against the advice of his managers, RA meant that literally.&#13; A Higher Peak&#13; In the winter of 2012, RA decided to climb Mount Kilimanjaro‚ÄîAfrica‚Äôs tallest peak. It was a challenge he‚Äôd dreamed of conquering ever since he read a Hemingway story about it as a teenager. RA was doing it for charity: he‚Äôd raised over $100,000 to rescue teenagers from sex trafficking in Bombay.&#13; The Mets tried to talk him out of it, and even sent a letter asserting their right to void his contract if he got hurt. Risking his full salary for the upcoming season, RA pressed forward. When he reached Tanzania, he spent seven days climbing over 16,000 feet with a group, battling altitude sickness, extreme exhaustion, and biting wind. Upon reaching the Kilimanjaro summit, ‚ÄúI somehow feel smaller than I have ever felt in my life,‚Äù he wrote. ‚ÄúIt is intoxicating.‚Äù&#13; That year, RA had the best season of his baseball career. Stretching beyond his comfort zone of the fast knuckleball, he developed one that was extremely slow, changing up speeds to leave batters guessing about when to swing as well as where. They would sometimes burst out laughing at how badly they missed the ball. He earned his nickname: the Baffler.&#13; At the ripe age of 37, RA achieved greater things. He made his first All-Star Game. He pitched back-to-back games in which he only gave up a single hit and set a Mets record for pitching 32 straight scoreless innings. He led the entire league in strikeouts and became the first knuckleballer ever to win the Cy Young Award for best pitcher.&#13; ‚ÄúDid Mt. Kilimanjaro Turn New York Mets Pitcher into an All-Star?‚Äù one reporter asked. It‚Äôs a provocative question. As a social scientist, I can confidently answer this question with a definitive maybe.&#13; The evidence on side hustles and hobbies suggests that the climb could have made a difference. But it also could‚Äôve just been a matter of chance. When I asked RA about it, though, he didn‚Äôt hesitate. ‚ÄúI don‚Äôt think it‚Äôs a coincidence,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúFor me, it was very important. I enjoyed challenging myself.‚Äù&#13; The detour to Kilimanjaro may have been the final charge for RA‚Äôs battery. Raising money for charity gave him a sense of contribution. Feeling small relieved the pressure to perform and refueled him to do bigger things. The successful climb gave him a burst of confidence. ‚ÄúIt was a quest,‚Äù he recounted. ‚ÄúI began the year on an African summit and I am ending it on a baseball summit.‚Äù&#13; To observers, the breakout season looked as if it came out of nowhere. But that couldn‚Äôt be further from the truth. It took RA seven trips down to the minors and seven years of knuckleball effort to become an overnight success. What looks like a big breakthrough is usually the accumulation of small wins.[*]&#13; When you get stuck on your way up a mountain, it‚Äôs better to shift into reverse than to stand still. As you take U-turns and detours, you‚Äôll feel as if you‚Äôre going in circles. In the short run, a straight line brings faster progress. But in the long run, loops lead to the highest peaks.&#13; Progress is rarely noticeable at a snapshot in time‚Äîit unfolds over extended periods of time. If you focus your attention on a specific difficult moment, it‚Äôs easy to feel stuck. It‚Äôs only when you look at your trajectory over the course of weeks, months, or years that you appreciate the distance you‚Äôve traveled.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13;

 &#13; Chapter 6&#13; Defying Gravity&#13; The Art of Flying by Our Bootstraps&#13; &#13; I believe in pulling yourself up by your own bootstraps. I believe it is possible.&#13; I saw this guy do it once in Cirque du Soleil.&#13; ‚ÄîStephen Colbert&#13; &#13; The mysterious messages came in different forms, but the recipients all felt the urgency. Jesse Arbor was in the midst of a poker game when someone told him a car was waiting downstairs and his train would leave in 35 minutes. He didn‚Äôt even have time to get some of his clothes out of the washing machine. James Hair was out on a tugboat when he was sent back to shore to retrieve a big brown envelope. As he broke the red wax seal, what he found inside wasn‚Äôt an invitation. It was an order to report to a location north of Chicago.&#13; That order was delivered to sixteen men around the country. They ranged from their mid 20s to mid 30s and came from varying walks of life‚Äîthe group included a mechanic, a bookbinder, a porter, a lawyer, and a sheet metal worker. It was January 1944, and they had no idea what was in store for them. In the heat of World War II, they would have a chance to make history. They were about to become the first Black men to enter officer training in the U.S. Navy.&#13; Among the branches of the military, the Navy was known to be particularly prejudiced. Just a quarter century earlier, the Navy had banned Black citizens from enlisting altogether. When the policy finally changed, Black men were limited to servile roles as cooks and shoe shiners. Now political pressure from Eleanor Roosevelt had cracked the door open for them to join the officer ranks, but many leaders doubted whether they were smart enough to command white sailors.&#13; When they arrived for officer training, the Black candidates were in their own cohort, segregated from the white sailors. They endured racial slurs and demeaning comments from the very instructors who were assigned to teach them. To them, the message was clear: they were expected to fail.&#13; Some of the men had additional reasons to doubt themselves. A few had struggled academically in the past. Jesse Arbor was a C student who had failed introductory economics, and Charles Lear hadn‚Äôt made it past tenth grade. And William ‚ÄúSyl‚Äù White had no military experience at all, having just finished his basic training at boot camp. ‚ÄúIt was demanding,‚Äù White recalled. ‚ÄúOfficer training was kind of like fighting in the dark.‚Äù&#13; To make matters worse, with the country at war the training period was cut in half. The candidates had to complete a full semester of coursework in just ten grueling weeks. They would wake up each day at 6 a.m. to march, take eight hours of classes, and study well into the night. They were tasked with mastering seamanship, navigation, gunnery, law, naval regulations, aircraft recognition, signaling with flags and Morse code, and survival‚Äîall in record time.&#13; In a typical Navy officer training class, only three quarters of candidates passed their exams. But that first class of Black officer candidates didn‚Äôt just scrape by‚Äîall sixteen of them soared far above the bar. Leaders in Washington were immediately suspicious. To prove they hadn‚Äôt cheated or benefited from grading errors, the men had to retake some of their exams. They ended up scoring even higher, finishing with a collective GPA of 3.89 out of 4.0. Years later, they would learn that they attained the highest marks in Navy history. Their potential was no longer hidden.&#13; Thirteen of the candidates ended up being called to serve as Navy officers.[*] As the first group of Black men in America to wear gold stars and stripes, they‚Äôre known as the Golden Thirteen. Instead of succumbing to the forces of gravity weighing them down, the Golden Thirteen managed to rise above them. As Samuel Barnes observed, ‚ÄúWe were determined to succeed in spite of the burden that was being placed on our shoulders.‚Äù&#13; The Golden Thirteen got something right that the rest of us often get wrong. In the face of seemingly insurmountable obstacles, it can be tempting to give up. It‚Äôs just too hard; the forces against us are just too strong. At times like this, we‚Äôre advised to pull ourselves up by our own bootstraps. The message is that we need to look inside ourselves for hidden reserves of confidence and know-how. But it‚Äôs actually in turning outward to harness resources with and for others that we discover‚Äîand develop‚Äîour hidden potential. When the odds are against us, focusing beyond ourselves is what launches us off the ground.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Schooling Each Other&#13; When we‚Äôre facing a daunting task, we need both competence and confidence. Our ability to elevate our skills and our expectations depends first on how we interpret the obstacles in front of us. Extensive evidence shows that when we view hurdles as threats, we tend to back down and give up. When we treat barriers as challenges to conquer, we rise to the occasion.&#13; Seeing obstacles as challenges depends partly on having a growth mindset‚Äîbelieving in your ability to improve. But the pioneering psychologist behind this idea, Carol Dweck, has recently demonstrated that a growth mindset alone does little good without scaffolding to support it. Rigorous experiments with over 15,000 students reveal that nurturing a growth mindset among high schoolers boosts their grades only when their teachers recognize their potential and their schools have cultures of embracing challenges.&#13; If we aren‚Äôt lucky enough to have others hand us that scaffolding, we may have to assemble it ourselves. That‚Äôs where bootstrapping comes in. Bootstrapping is using your existing resources to pull yourself out of a sticky situation. The term is sometimes traced to a folktale about a baron who was stuck in a swamp with his horse‚Äîand escaped by using his pigtails as a rope. Later tellings may have replaced the baron‚Äôs hair with his bootstraps.&#13; Bootstrapping is normally seen as an individual skill. You don‚Äôt rely on assistance from others. You pull at the loop at the back of your own boots to lift yourself over obstacles. It sounds like an expression of rugged individualism‚Äîan independent act. But it‚Äôs when we approach it interdependently that we gain the competence and the confidence to overcome obstacles. I saw it happen in my own classroom.&#13; One fall, I told my students at Wharton that the final exam would be unusually difficult and sent them some sample questions. They came to class panicked about the multiple-choice section. I‚Äôd wanted to motivate them to master the material, but I‚Äôd undermined their confidence.&#13; I reminded them that I was rooting for all of them to succeed. I even promised to curve their scores upward if the class average was low. They were still stressed and filled with doubts, so I decided to throw them a bone. For the one multiple-choice question that stumped them, they could write the name of a classmate who might know the answer. If that student got it right, they would get credit too. It was the academic equivalent of a lifeline in Who Wants to Be a Millionaire.&#13; When I looked at their scores on the exam, I was in for a surprise. Compared to the past year‚Äôs exam, the class average had gone up by multiple points, and it wasn‚Äôt due to the lifeline. In subsequent years, the performance of each new class continued to climb. I started cycling through the list of possible explanations. The test hadn‚Äôt gotten easier. The students weren‚Äôt smarter than previous years. Eventually, I found out what was going on: the small lifeline had a big impact on how students prepared.&#13; To excel on the exam, the students still had to master all the content‚Äîit wasn‚Äôt as simple as dividing and conquering the material. But if they wanted the extra credit on one question, they had to find out who knew what. So instead of cramming solo, they opted to study together. They started meeting in small groups to synthesize the key concepts.&#13; The students had created their own scaffolding. A later class took the collaboration to the next level: they created a giant map of the content from the entire semester. One student reserved a room for Saturday afternoon studying and invited the entire class to join and pool their knowledge. Others jumped in to share their own reading summaries, study guides, and practice quizzes. The students realized that the strongest bootstraps weren‚Äôt the ones they created alone, but the ones they built together.&#13; Considerable evidence shows that studying with knowledgeable colleagues is good for growth. In U.S. intelligence agencies, if you want to predict which teams will produce the best work, the most important factor to consider is how often colleagues teach and coach one another. In medical schools, students learn as much when they‚Äôre taught by peers as by faculty. In the Saturday session in my class, no single student walked in an expert. They had to leverage the collective knowledge of the group. And there‚Äôs reason to believe that they did some of their learning by teaching.&#13; Teaching is a surprisingly powerful method of learning. In a meta-analysis of 16 studies, students who were randomly assigned to tutor their peers ended up earning higher scores in the material they were teaching. Students who taught reading improved in reading; those who taught math got dramatically better at math. The more time they spent tutoring, the more they learned. As one group of researchers concluded, ‚ÄúLike the children they helped, the tutors gained a better understanding of and developed more positive attitudes toward the subject matter covered in the tutorial program.‚Äù [*]&#13; Psychologists call this the tutor effect. It‚Äôs even effective for novices: the best way to learn something is to teach it. You remember it better after you recall it‚Äîand you understand it better after you explain it. All it takes is embracing the discomfort of putting yourself in the instructor‚Äôs seat before you‚Äôve reached mastery. Even just being told you‚Äôre going to teach something is enough to boost your learning.&#13; This is another twist on the trope that those who can‚Äôt do, teach. Those who can‚Äôt do yet can learn by teaching. The historian Henry Adams became an expert in medieval history by teaching a class on it. He confessed to his students that he knew nothing at the start and progressed just one lecture ahead of them. The painter Georgia O‚ÄôKeeffe honed her techniques of abstraction in charcoal and watercolor as she taught art classes. The physicist John Preskill learned quantum computing by signing up to deliver a course in it. And the Golden Thirteen aced their Navy officer exams by teaching what they wanted to learn.&#13; Unexpected Potential&#13; At the outset of officer training, many members of the Golden Thirteen felt it would be impossible to accumulate so much knowledge in so little time. As George Cooper captured it, ‚ÄúEach of us . . . said, ‚ÄòThe hell with it, this is just too damn much.‚Äô ‚Äù With few leaders supporting them, their best hope was to support one another. But they would have to do so in a notoriously cutthroat environment.&#13; Since Navy tradition held that not everyone would make the cut, officer candidates tended to see one another as rivals, not teammates. Yet the Golden Thirteen met in their barracks and made a prosocial vow: All for one and one for all. ‚ÄúWe decided early in the game that we were all going to either sink or swim together,‚Äù Cooper stated. ‚ÄúFortunately, at least one of us was already familiar with almost every subject we were exposed to.‚Äù&#13; To manage the overwhelming course load, the Golden Thirteen resolved to rely on one another. They would become sponges by pooling and filtering the knowledge in the room. Each member would teach his expertise to the rest of the group. When they got their textbooks, they went through the topics and waited for someone to shout out, ‚ÄúThat‚Äôs mine.‚Äù&#13; Being strong in math, Cooper, Graham Martin, and Reginald Goodwin led many of the analytical subjects along with John Reagan on technical topics. The history buffs, Samuel Barnes and Dennis Nelson, took charge on military history. What Lear lacked in formal education, he made up in leadership as well as experience with seamanship and knot-tying, so he chipped in there along with Alves. And since Arbor had advanced navigation training and experience, the group turned to him for a crash course on Morse code‚Äîhe tapped on the wall and gave his peers clues as they tapped back. They were tying their bootstraps together.&#13; The official time for lights out was 10:30 p.m. Night after night, the Golden Thirteen huddled in the bathroom with flashlights studying well past midnight. To make sure they wouldn‚Äôt get caught, they hung sheets over the windows to block the light.&#13; I learned about the Golden Thirteen thanks to a seminal book by Paul Stillwell, a Navy historian who did the great service of documenting their experiences before they passed away. As I pored over his extensive interviews for stories, quotes, and insights, the obvious explanation for their success was that they were able to learn from qualified peers. Then it occurred to me that the tutor effect was also at play: the act of sharing knowledge enhanced the competence of the men doing the teaching. They were working together to build stronger bootstraps.&#13; When the first law question came up, everyone turned to White as the lawyer. He told them he had no knowledge of Navy law‚Äîhe had to look it up. He learned Navy regulations by teaching them‚Äîand gained efficacy in subjects he had just learned in boot camp by teaching some of those too. Serving as an instructor made the lessons stick.&#13; Drawing on his background as a machinist, Frank Sublett was responsible for mechanical, gunnery, and boilers along with Dalton Baugh. But plenty of questions came up that they couldn‚Äôt answer. When the appointed teacher didn‚Äôt have relevant knowledge, the Golden Thirteen went around the room for everyone to weigh in. It gave them all the chance to teach something relatively new to them. Once they‚Äôd reached a consensus about the best explanation, it was time for ‚Äúdrilling‚Äù and ‚Äúgrilling.‚Äù By quizzing one another, they created more opportunities for everyone to share knowledge they‚Äôd just gained. As he saw that they each had insights to offer, Sublett realized that ‚Äúthe men in our group had the capacity and ability to grasp the information.‚Äù Their boots were sturdy. By the time they took their exams, they each had the knowledge to pass every subject with flying colors.&#13; Heeding Our Own Advice&#13; Teaching others can build our competence. But it‚Äôs coaching others that elevates our confidence. When we encourage others to overcome obstacles, it can help us find our own motivation. I‚Äôve seen it happen as a parent.&#13; When I was nervous about giving my first TED talk, I decided to ask our oldest daughter for advice. At the time, Joanna was an extremely shy eight-year-old, and she told me to look for one person in the audience who was smiling and nodding. I came home excited to tell her I found encouragement from a beaming face in the front row.&#13; A few weeks later, my wife, Allison, and I could sense that Joanna was anxious about her role in a school play. When she walked onstage, she locked eyes with us in the audience and broke out into a grin as big as ours. Instead of relying on us for advice, she had remembered her own advice and followed it. It was a turning point: we watched her expectations of herself rise afterward. It wasn‚Äôt long before she was volunteering to give presentations, and her teachers were praising her poise and confidence.&#13; I‚Äôve come to think of this as the coach effect. We‚Äôre more confident in our ability to surmount struggles after guiding others through them. That‚Äôs the consistent result of elegant experiments led by the psychologist Lauren Eskreis-Winkler. In one experiment, high schoolers earned higher report-card grades in multiple subjects‚Äîincluding math‚Äîafter being randomly assigned to give advice to younger students on how to stay motivated and avoid procrastinating. In another, middle schoolers spent more time on homework after they were randomly assigned to give motivational advice to younger students‚Äîrather than receive motivational advice from expert teachers. And people who were struggling to save money, lose weight, control their tempers, and find jobs all came away more motivated after giving advice than receiving it.&#13; This is different from the tutor effect, which highlights how we can learn through sharing the very knowledge that we want to acquire. The coach effect captures how we can marshal motivation by offering the encouragement to others that we need for ourselves. By reminding us of the tools we already possess, coaching others raises our expectations of ourselves.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Coaching is the opposite of what we usually do when we need help. In trying times, our first instinct is to pick up the phone and ask for advice. We‚Äôre better off pausing to reflect on the advice we‚Äôve provided in the past or calling someone in a similar situation and offering them some suggestions. We should listen to the advice we give to others‚Äîit‚Äôs usually the advice we need to take for ourselves.&#13; In my research, I‚Äôve found that it‚Äôs more motivating to be a giver than a receiver. Receiving is passive‚Äîif you‚Äôre always the one being coached, it puts you in the position of depending on others for guidance. Giving is active‚Äîcoaching others reminds you that you have something to offer. It convinces you that your bootstraps are strong enough to support you. You‚Äôve already seen them support others.&#13; Throughout the winter, the Golden Thirteen leaned on one another for coaching and encouragement. To rally the group, Lear showed up early. When they struggled with learning to use a sextant for navigation, he motivated them to keep trying and guided them on techniques. It showed him‚Äîand his peers‚Äîthat he had a contribution to make.&#13; In coaching one another, the Golden Thirteen didn‚Äôt just offer advice and encouragement. They also held one another accountable. ‚ÄúEach one of us, at one point in time or maybe more than one, got to the point where we were ready to throw in the towel,‚Äù George Cooper recounted. ‚ÄúAnd the others would have to be there to say, ‚ÄòOh, no, man, we must do this.‚Äô ‚Äù Graham Martin told them they had to make the grade and shot down excuses for slacking off. Others called their peers out if they got distracted and exchanged tips for staying focused. As they pulled one another up by their bootstraps, their expectations of themselves rose.&#13; After interviewing most of the Golden Thirteen, Paul Stillwell marveled at their response: ‚ÄúEven their toughest instructors weren‚Äôt as demanding of them as they were of themselves.‚Äù The difficult task in front of them was no longer a threat‚Äîit was a challenge. Instead of doubting themselves individually, they believed in their collective capacity. As George Cooper articulated it, ‚ÄúWe were convinced that if one of us made it, we were all going to make it.‚Äù&#13; The Golden Thirteen found a way to fill the initial gaps in their confidence and competence. But with leaders doubting them, there would be plenty of reasons ahead for them to question themselves. When other people don‚Äôt believe in your abilities, it can tie your boots to the ground or send you running in the other direction. Being disrespected presents a particular barrier to growth and requires a particular kind of scaffolding to overcome.&#13; Recently, I came across someone who navigated that challenge. For years, I‚Äôd been hearing about her as the speaker who‚Äôd electrified the crowd before I got onstage. When I finally met her, she taught me something surprising about finding motivation in an uphill battle.&#13; A Mountain of Doubt&#13; As she gasped for air, Alison Levine wondered if she had made a mistake. It was 2002, and Alison was captaining the first expedition by a group of American women up Mount Everest‚Äîthe highest peak on earth. Alison had reached the most treacherous part of the climb: the Khumbu Icefall. Above her were 2,000 vertical feet of ice, which would become increasingly unstable as the sun‚Äôs heat intensified. She needed to move quickly to avoid the possibility of a crack in the ice or a sudden avalanche.&#13; But speed was not Alison‚Äôs forte. At just 5‚Äô4‚Äù and 112 pounds, she lacked the size and strength to take long, powerful steps. She was in her mid 30s, and she had only started climbing a few years earlier. As a child in the sweltering summers of Phoenix, she devoured books about Arctic explorers and watched every movie she could find about mountain climbers. It was her dream to go on polar expeditions, but physical limitations prevented her from braving the cold.&#13; Alison was born with a hole in her heart; a career as an expeditioner was not supposed to be in the cards. She spent her teenage years sporadically passing out and getting shuttled back and forth to the emergency room. After multiple surgeries, the hole was finally closed, and she was finally cleared to climb. But she still faced a major barrier: a circulatory disorder. In frigid temperatures, her arteries would stop sending blood to her fingers and toes, leaving them numb and at severe risk of frostbite.&#13; That hadn‚Äôt kept Alison from making the trek to Everest. She‚Äôd spent months recruiting a corporate sponsor and assembling a team of women who were accomplished outdoor athletes. Now they stood together, staring at a giant hole in a glacier that they needed to cross. If they slipped, they could fall to their deaths.&#13; As Alison put her foot on the ladder, she heard a voice behind her. ‚ÄúYou‚Äôre never going to make it to the summit at this pace,‚Äù a male climber shouted. ‚ÄúIf you can‚Äôt go faster, you shouldn‚Äôt be here. Maybe you should call it quits and go home.‚Äù Doing her best to ignore him, she made her way, slowly but surely.&#13; She and her team eventually cleared the Icefall. Shortly afterward, a section collapsed into an avalanche, and another climber narrowly made it out alive. That wouldn‚Äôt be their only brush with death. A helicopter that transported the team through the Khumbu Valley crashed on its return to a nearby mountain, leaving no survivors. And one of the climbers they had met at base camp had later slipped and fallen to his death. Alison knew their lives were hanging in the balance of countless tiny decisions and conditions beyond their control.&#13; After nearly two months of climbing, Alison and her teammates made it to the last stretch of the journey. It was known as the death zone‚Äîat that altitude most humans couldn‚Äôt absorb enough oxygen to survive. Even with supplemental oxygen, Alison had to take five to ten breaths just to get enough oxygen to take a single step. As they pressed forward, the peak was finally in sight.&#13; Then a storm blew in. Between heavy winds and a whiteout, it was too dangerous to proceed or even wait it out. After climbing nearly 29,000 feet, they were forced to quit . . . less than 300 feet from the top. Instead of becoming the first American women to summit Everest, they turned around. Alison led them back down the mountain.&#13; As soon as she got home, she had to face a slew of questions from reporters. Congratulations, you didn‚Äôt make it. How did it feel to give up? Against her better judgment, Alison read the online comments. They weren‚Äôt qualified. They didn‚Äôt deserve to be there. She met people at dinner parties who belittled her efforts. Stop saying you climbed Everest . . . if you didn‚Äôt get to the top, it doesn‚Äôt count. She felt like a failure.&#13; Alison sank into a spiral of self-doubt and depression. She felt she‚Äôd let her team, her sponsor, and her country down. She kept hearing the voice of the climber behind her echoing in her head. You‚Äôre not fast enough. She swore she would never set foot on Everest again.&#13; Lighting a Spark&#13; The expectations people hold of us often become self-fulfilling prophecies. When others believe in our potential, they give us a ladder. They elevate our aspirations and enable us to reach higher peaks. Dozens of experiments show that at work, when leaders hold high expectations, employees generally work harder, learn more, and perform better. In schools, when teachers set high expectations, students get smarter and earn higher grades‚Äîespecially if they start out with disadvantages.&#13; Whereas high expectations offer support for us to climb, low expectations tend to hold us back‚Äîit feels like our boots are made of lead. It‚Äôs called the Golem effect: when others underestimate us, it limits our effort and growth. These kinds of self-fulfilling prophecies are particularly pronounced among stigmatized groups, who are frequently inundated by low expectations. But groundbreaking research by my colleague Samir Nurmohamed offers a twist. There are times when you can turn others‚Äô low expectations to your advantage. They don‚Äôt have to strap you in place‚Äîyou can grab on to them and pull yourself forward.&#13; In one experiment, Samir gave people a task where they had to click moving circles with their mouse. After they finished a practice round, an observer sent them a message. Some people were randomly assigned to receive high expectations: You will blow others away in this task. . . . I think you‚Äôve got what it takes to beat all of them. Others got low expectations: Others will blow you away in this task. . . . I don‚Äôt think you‚Äôve got what it takes to beat any of them.&#13; The impact of these expectations depended on who was setting them. High expectations led to greater effort and performance . . . if they came from someone knowledgeable about the task. But if the observer lacked credibility, being uninformed about the task, the effect reversed: people actually ended up trying harder and doing better when they were doubted rather than encouraged.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Credibility&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Ignorant&#13; &#13; &#13; Knowledgeable&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Expectations&#13; &#13; &#13; Doubter&#13; &#13; &#13; Challenged:&#13; I will prove them wrong&#13; &#13; &#13; Threatened:&#13; I don‚Äôt have what it takes&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Believer&#13; &#13; &#13; Unmoved:&#13; They‚Äôre easily impressed&#13; &#13; &#13; Empowered:&#13; I will prove them right&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; When you‚Äôre invested in a goal, being doubted by experts is a threat. They may be credible, but since they don‚Äôt recognize your potential, they‚Äôre not coaches who will help you improve. Their disbelief quickly becomes your insecurity. It shatters your confidence and stifles your growth. That‚Äôs a self-fulfilling prophecy.&#13; But the research suggests that when they come from an uninformed audience, low expectations can become a self-negating prophecy. You‚Äôre motivated to shatter their confidence that you won‚Äôt succeed. Samir calls it the underdog effect.&#13; Being doubted by novices is a challenge. It fires you up. They‚Äôre clueless, so you don‚Äôt internalize their low expectations‚Äîbut you don‚Äôt ignore them either. You become driven to defy them. I‚Äôll show you. The doubts that threaten to crush your confidence can become crucibles that fortify it. You feel like an underdog who can beat the odds.[*] Just ask Alison Levine.&#13; After failing to reach the summit of Mount Everest in 2002, Alison couldn‚Äôt get the naysayers out of her head. She was well aware that they weren‚Äôt credible. The online trolls, journalists, and acquaintances didn‚Äôt know anything about mountaineering. The climber on her heels had no idea what it took to scale a peak at her size and weight. ‚ÄúMy fear of not making it again was not as strong as my desire to prove the naysayers wrong,‚Äù Alison tells me. ‚ÄúWhen someone ignorant doubts you, it feels like a challenge. I didn‚Äôt want to let the naysayers win. I wanted to make them eat their words.‚Äù&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Our biggest challenges require scaffolding beyond the underdog effect. For Alison, the drive to put the critics in their place wasn‚Äôt enough to tip her over the edge to embrace the discomfort of returning to Everest. If she pulled on her bootstraps again, they might snap. A second failed attempt could be the end of her climbing career. She might never attract another team or another sponsor. She needed a bigger reason to take the risk.&#13; Carrying a Torch&#13; The desire to prove others wrong can light a spark of motivation. Turning the spark into a flame, though, often requires more. Ignorant naysayers may give us something to fight against, but a roaring fire comes from having something to fight for.&#13; It‚Äôs easier to overcome obstacles when we‚Äôre carrying a torch for people who matter to us. When others are counting on us, we find strength we didn‚Äôt know we had. In one study, Marissa Shandell and I compared the performance of Olympic divers when they were competing individually versus in synchronized events with a partner. Divers were less likely to choke on the exact same difficult dives when they were doing it synchro rather than solo. You can see a similar pattern with kids in a version of the marshmallow test. In experiments in Germany and Kenya, kids had the chance to eat one cookie now or wait a few minutes to be rewarded with a second cookie. They delayed gratification longer when they knew caving in would also deprive another child of an extra cookie. Having a partner can prevent rumination about your own abilities (Can I do this?) and boost determination (I won‚Äôt be the reason you fail). As Maya Angelou wrote, ‚ÄúI do my best because I‚Äôm counting on you counting on me.‚Äù&#13; After her failed Everest expedition, Alison Levine had someone counting on her. Her friend Meg urged her to try again. ‚ÄúOnly if you go with me,‚Äù Alison responded, knowing it was impossible. Although Meg was an elite athlete, two bouts of lymphoma had damaged her lungs.&#13; Tragically, Meg passed away in 2009 due to a lung infection. Alison wanted to do something meaningful to honor her legacy, so she decided to climb Everest in Meg‚Äôs memory. That was something worth fighting for. In a matter of months, she had carved Meg‚Äôs name on her ice axe and booked a flight to Nepal to join an expedition with mountaineers she didn‚Äôt know.
