You‚Äôre not stuck with eight different bosses breathing down your neck like in Office Space. You don‚Äôt have multiple managers holding you back and shooting you down. The goal is to give you access to multiple leaders who are willing and able to help move you forward and lift you up.&#13; The best example I‚Äôve seen of a lattice system is at W. L. Gore, the company known for making waterproof Gore-Tex gloves and jackets. Back in the mid-1990s, a rank-and-file medical device engineer at Gore named Dave Myers figured out that coating the gear cables on his mountain bike with Gore-Tex protected them from grit. It dawned on him that Gore-Tex might also be useful for repelling the grit from human hands that leads guitar strings to lose their tone over time.&#13; Even though it wasn‚Äôt part of his day job, Dave took the initiative to mock up a prototype. He brought it to some senior people, but they didn‚Äôt think it was worth pursuing. They had technical objections. You can‚Äôt coat a vibrating string with fluoropolymer‚Äîyou‚Äôll ruin the sound! They had strategic concerns too. We‚Äôre not in the music business‚Äîwhy would we make guitar strings?&#13; In a typical organization, those protests would have been enough to squash the idea. But Gore has a lattice system. Whenever you have an idea, you‚Äôre granted the freedom to go to a range of different senior people. To get your project off the ground, all you need is one leader who‚Äôs willing to sponsor it. So Dave kept socializing his idea. Eventually he found a sponsor, Richie Snyder, who connected him with an engineer named John Spencer.&#13; For the next year, Dave and John dedicated part of every work week to their unproven idea. Instead of seeing those kinds of side projects as diverting or disobedient, Gore encouraged them‚Äîthey gave people ‚Äúdabble time‚Äù to tinker. To make headway on the guitar strings, Dave and John didn‚Äôt need Richie‚Äôs formal approval. They just gave him regular updates as they developed and tested prototypes with thousands of musicians.&#13; A lattice system rejects two unwritten rules that dominate ladder hierarchies: don‚Äôt go behind your boss‚Äôs back or above your boss‚Äôs head. Amy Edmondson‚Äôs research suggests that these implicit rules stop many people from speaking up and being heard. The purpose of a lattice system is to remove the punishment for going around and above the boss.&#13; At Gore, Dave and John weren‚Äôt shy about going above Richie when they needed ideas and support. They took advantage of the leeway to contact anyone at any time. At one point, they even had the chairman-CEO himself dropping into their meetings and giving advice.&#13; It took Dave, John, and their makeshift team 18 months to develop and launch the product. Just 15 months later, their Elixir strings became the market leader in strings for acoustic guitars. It‚Äôs not every day that an idea hatched in a medical products division makes a dent in the music industry. But it did, thanks to the lattice system.&#13; Even when organizational charts look like ladders, it‚Äôs possible to design ad hoc lattice systems to surface and propel fledgling ideas. I see these systems often in innovation tournaments‚Äîcontests to generate novel solutions to problems. Dow Chemical once issued an internal call for proposals to save energy and reduce waste, offering to sponsor the most promising ideas from employees that cost no more than $200,000 and had the potential to pay for themselves within a year. Over the next decade, they bet on 575 ideas, which saved the company an average of $110 million a year.&#13; In this kind of innovation tournament, decisions aren‚Äôt up to individual gatekeepers. A lattice system engages people at different levels to do peer reviews of the submissions. This prevents entries from being shut down prematurely or unfairly, ensuring that every idea gets due consideration.&#13; Weak leaders silence voice and shoot the messenger. Strong leaders welcome voice and thank the messenger. Great leaders build systems to amplify voice and elevate the messenger.&#13; Light at the Bottom of the Tunnel&#13; When I first learned about how Igor Proestakis pitched his plan B, I immediately recognized the hallmarks of a lattice system. It was why he was able to bring his idea to the geologists supervising the drilling‚Äîand why they took him straight to the top despite his youth and inexperience. But the benefits of the lattice system became even more visible about a month into the rescue initiative.&#13; Plan A was moving even slower than anticipated, and plan B was looking better and better. Igor had been right about the cluster hammer: it was smashing swiftly and smoothly through the rock. It had a clear advantage . . . until it stopped working altogether. A third of the way down, it kept spinning but stopped cutting.&#13; It turned out that they‚Äôd hit a series of iron rods that were in place to reinforce the mine, which had shattered the hammer‚Äôs drill bit into pieces. One of those pieces‚Äîa hunk of metal the size of a basketball‚Äîwas now blocking the hole they were expanding to reach the miners. The team tried to bash the metal out and pull it out with magnets, but it wouldn‚Äôt budge. They were ready to give up on Igor‚Äôs plan B.&#13; The next day, Igor had another idea. He remembered an extraction tool he‚Äôd read about in school‚Äîan industrial version of an arcade claw machine. My wife, Allison, is the master of that machine, but we had no idea it served a purpose higher than winning plush teddy bears. It was exactly the kind of approach they needed. If they could lower a metal jaw and clamp its teeth around the obstructing piece of metal, they could extract it and clear the hole.&#13; When Igor first suggested the claw machine to a few people on-site, they didn‚Äôt listen. But due to the lattice system, he knew he had other routes to getting his idea heard. After two days of having his idea ignored at lower levels, Igor managed to get an audience with the Chilean mining minister himself, who gave it the green light right away. For the next five days, they tried and failed until it finally captured the broken drill bit, lifted it out, and opened the hole. Best claw prize ever. To everyone‚Äôs relief, plan B was back on track.&#13; Late one evening the following month, a rescue worker went down through that hole in a capsule. Shortly before midnight, the capsule came back up carrying the first miner. And less than 24 hours later, the rescue capsule carried the foreman‚Äîthe last of the 33 miners‚Äîout through the hole.&#13; Igor‚Äôs claw idea saved his plan B‚Äîand his idea for plan B helped save 33 lives. There‚Äôs no question that we should applaud the creative, heroic efforts from him and so many others. But let‚Äôs not forget the unsung heroes of this story: the leadership practices, team processes, and systems of opportunity that made it possible for people to speak up and be heard.&#13; If we listen only to the smartest person in the room, we miss out on discovering the smarts that the rest of the room has to offer. Our greatest potential isn‚Äôt always hidden inside us‚Äîsometimes it sparks between us, and sometimes it comes from outside our team altogether.&#13; &#13;

 &#13; Chapter 9&#13; Diamonds in the Rough&#13; Discovering Uncut Gems in Job Interviews and College Admissions&#13; &#13; Success is to be measured not so much by the position that one has reached in life&#13; as by the obstacles . . . overcome while trying to succeed.&#13; ‚ÄîBooker T. Washington&#13; &#13; On a historic evening in 1972, ten-year-old Jos√© Hernandez kneeled in front of an old black-and-white television. He gripped the rabbit ears of the antenna, using his body to boost the signal. As the fuzzy image on the screen became clearer, Jos√© watched the last Apollo astronauts bound across the surface of the moon.&#13; Jos√© was mesmerized by the moonwalk, but he yearned for an even better view. He unglued his eyes from the screen, raced outside to look at the moon, and ran back in time to see one of the astronauts take his final giant leap. Jos√© hoped that one day, he could etch his own footsteps alongside theirs in the lunar dirt.&#13; Many kids go through an astronaut phase, but Jos√© was committed to making his dream a reality. Since his strongest subjects were math and science, he decided engineering would be his ride to space. Over the next two decades, he earned a bachelor‚Äôs and master‚Äôs degree in electrical engineering and landed a job as an engineer at a federal research facility. He wanted to make his application as strong as possible for NASA.&#13; In 1989, Jos√© was ready to throw his hat in the ring. He carefully filled out the 47 sections of the astronaut application, enclosed his resume and transcripts, and shipped his packet off to Houston. Soon he was checking his mailbox daily, eagerly awaiting an envelope from NASA. After ten long months, it finally arrived. He ripped it open and read the letter from the head of the astronaut selection office. Not selected.&#13; Jos√© wasn‚Äôt fazed. His aspirations were high, but his expectations were modest‚Äîhe knew the odds were long. He took the initiative to call NASA for feedback, and followed up with a letter asking how he could improve:&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; NASA got back to him with disappointing news. Since he hadn‚Äôt made it past the initial screening, they didn‚Äôt have notes or advice for him to absorb. Undeterred, he applied again . . . and was rejected again.&#13; Jos√© didn‚Äôt give up hope. He kept putting himself back in the ring‚Äîrevising his resume, highlighting his strengths, updating his references as he reapplied‚Äîonly to be met with rejection after rejection. He couldn‚Äôt even get his foot in the door for an interview.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; In 1996, the last rejection broke his spirit. Jos√© had the sinking feeling that he would never be enough for NASA. He crumpled the letter into a ball and threw it at the garbage can. He was so disappointed that when it missed, he just left it on the floor.&#13; In life, there are few things more consequential than the judgments people make of our potential. When colleges evaluate students for admission and employers interview applicants for jobs, they‚Äôre making forecasts about future success. These predictions can become gateways to opportunity. Whether the door swings open or slams shut hangs in the balance of their assessments.&#13; What Jos√© didn‚Äôt know was that none of his applications even registered a blip on NASA‚Äôs radar. They were looking for people with operational experience making decisions in high-stress environments. They expected to see noteworthy accomplishments by engineers. They took notice of applicants who graduated at the top of their class. NASA was focused on finding people who had already achieved great things, and by their standards, that wasn‚Äôt Jos√©. But what NASA‚Äôs process failed to capture‚Äîas so many organizations do‚Äîwas a candidate‚Äôs potential for doing greater things.&#13; In the time between application cycles, Jos√© had developed and demonstrated the unusual combination of technical, physical, and character skills that NASA supposedly prized. With a mentor at work, he won a government grant and developed a digital cancer detection technology that helped save many lives. In his spare time, he ran seven marathons with a personal best under three hours‚Äî26.2 miles at a pace under seven minutes per mile. Along with being disciplined and determined, he was prosocial: he volunteered to tutor high school students in math, started a chapter of a professional society for Mexican American scientists and engineers, and served his community in a series of local and national leadership roles. Each time he reapplied to be an astronaut, he highlighted new accomplishments, but they didn‚Äôt make a dent.&#13; NASA missed the markers of Jos√©‚Äôs potential because their selection process wasn‚Äôt designed to detect them. They had information about work experience and past performance, not life experience and background. They didn‚Äôt know that Jos√© was raised in a family of migrant farmworkers. They didn‚Äôt know that when he started kindergarten in California, he didn‚Äôt speak English, and it wasn‚Äôt until he turned twelve that he finally felt fluent. They didn‚Äôt know that he had traveled a great distance just to make it to college and become an engineer. The lack of accomplishments in his early applications seemed to reveal the absence of ability, but it actually indicated the presence of adversity.&#13; It‚Äôs a mistake to judge people solely by the heights they‚Äôve reached. By favoring applicants who have already excelled, selection systems underestimate and overlook candidates who are capable of greater things. When we confuse past performance with future potential, we miss out on people whose achievements have involved overcoming major obstacles. We need to consider how steep their slope was, how far they‚Äôve climbed, and how they‚Äôve grown along the way. The test of a diamond in the rough is not whether it shines from the start, but how it responds to heat or pressure.&#13; The Sorting Hat Is Out of Sorts&#13; For much of human history, opportunity was a privilege of birthright. If you had noble blood, the world was your oyster. If you weren‚Äôt from the right background, your lot was already cast and your options were limited. Across centuries in many cultures, this dynamic has transformed as people have challenged monarchies, aristocracies, and caste systems. In Confucian China, successive dynasties began opening government posts to anyone who could pass a difficult civil service exam. They still had a long way to go, because women and people with disabilities were prohibited from taking the exam. In ancient Greece, Socrates and Plato proposed that societies should be ruled by philosopher kings who earned their wisdom by studying. Their intent was not merely to reimagine how people are chosen to lead; it was to pave the way for new social orders that rewarded individuals for agency and ability.&#13; Today, opening doors to anyone qualified is a high priority for universities and employers. In principle, application processes invite people from a wide range of backgrounds to show what they‚Äôre capable of. But in practice, our systems for judging qualifications are flawed.&#13; In schools and workplaces, selection systems are usually designed to detect excellence. That means people who are on their way to excellence rarely make the cut. We don‚Äôt pay enough attention to these people and their paths‚Äîwhich are often filled with speed bumps and roadblocks. When we fail to see hidden potential, along with shattering people‚Äôs dreams, we lose out on their contributions.&#13; Our errors in identifying potential come at multiple stages of the assessment process as we struggle to contend with limited time and large applicant pools. During an initial screen, it‚Äôs impossible to really get to know every candidate. There isn‚Äôt an algorithm for spotting diamonds in the rough, and there aren‚Äôt enough hours in the day to do a deep dive into each person‚Äôs life history. Evaluators end up making life-altering decisions for candidates who have been reduced to thin slices of information.&#13; In the first stages of hiring, employers try to address this challenge by relying on credentials. The assumption is that the best colleges admit and produce the best candidates. Yet pedigrees aren‚Äôt all they‚Äôre cracked up to be. In a study of over 28,000 students, those who attended higher-ranking universities performed only slightly better than their peers on consulting projects. If you look at the quality of their work and their contributions as collaborators, a Yale student was just 1.9 percent better than a Cleveland State student. And if you require a bachelor‚Äôs degree, you lose out on over half the American workforce. This systematically disadvantages candidates who acquire skills through alternative routes‚Äîat trade schools or two-year community colleges, through apprenticeships or military service, or by teaching themselves or learning on the job.&#13; Beyond college degrees, many managers turn to prior experience to get an initial sense of candidates‚Äô qualifications. But it turns out that the amount of experience is also borderline irrelevant. In a meta-analysis of 44 studies with over 11,000 people across a wide range of jobs, prior work experience had virtually no bearing on performance. A candidate with 20 years of experience on a resume may have just repeated the same year of experience 20 times. So, you need experience to get a job, but you need a job to get experience . . . and that experience reveals little about your potential.[*] The key question is not how long people have done a job. It‚Äôs how well they can learn to do a job.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; To decide which candidates to advance to the next round, many employers look at prior performance. Compared to credentials and prior work experience, this is a better clue to potential. How well someone has performed in the past can give us a sense of their ability in the present. But this metric also has drawbacks that lead us to overlook the potential in too many people.&#13; Past performance is only helpful if the new job requires similar skills to the old one. In a study of over 38,000 salespeople, economists found that the most successful salespeople were more likely to be promoted to manager. But sales skills aren‚Äôt the same as managerial skills‚Äîthe candidates who were better at closing deals were worse at managing people. It turned out that the managers who elevated a team‚Äôs performance weren‚Äôt the biggest rainmakers, but the most prosocial members‚Äîas indicated by how often they‚Äôd made collaborative sales with their colleagues.&#13; This is an example of a phenomenon known as the Peter Principle. It‚Äôs the idea that people at work tend to get promoted to their ‚Äúlevel of incompetence‚Äù‚Äîthey keep advancing based on their success in previous jobs until they get trapped in a new role that‚Äôs beyond their abilities. In this case, the best salespeople went on to become incompetent managers, and the best potential managers got stuck as mediocre salespeople.[*]&#13; Even if a candidate‚Äôs past performance is relevant to the current role, this metric is designed to detect polished diamonds, not uncut gems. Take Tom Brady: whether you love him or hate him, he‚Äôs widely regarded as the greatest quarterback in football history. But when he entered the NFL, he wasn‚Äôt drafted until the 199th pick. Based on his performance in college and in the draft combine, scouts had serious doubts about whether Brady‚Äôs arm was strong enough to zip a spiral and hurl a Hail Mary. It was unlikely that he would be fast enough to escape a blitz.&#13; The problem is that scouts were focusing on Brady‚Äôs body, not his mind. They were right about his physical limitations: despite weighing only 211 pounds, Brady was outrun by 25 different linemen who tipped the scales at 300+ pounds. But scouts didn‚Äôt account for what journalists would later call his ‚Äúnerves of steel.‚Äù They ‚Äúdidn‚Äôt open up his chest and look at his heart,‚Äù one coach lamented. It‚Äôs often said that talent sets the floor, but character sets the ceiling. Brady went on to break through the assumed ceilings: the year he turned 40, he beat his 40-yard dash time from when he was 20. Of course, when you‚Äôre as slow as Tom Brady, the floor is pretty low.&#13; If natural talent determines where people start, learned character affects how far they go. But character skills aren‚Äôt always immediately apparent. If we don‚Äôt look beyond the surface, we risk missing the potential for brilliance beneath.&#13; Uncut Gems&#13; When I wanted to find out how to identify hidden potential, I knew NASA was an ideal organization to study. The stakes are sky-high: picking the wrong astronaut could jeopardize a mission and cost crews their lives. That left the agency much more concerned about false positives (accepting bad candidates) than false negatives (rejecting good ones).&#13; To understand why we miss potential and how we can spot it, I reached out to Duane Ross. He led astronaut selection at NASA for four decades, signing the rejection letters personally by hand‚Äîincluding each of Jos√©‚Äôs. I wanted to learn about the process of sifting through the dreams of thousands of applicants to put the future of space exploration in the hands of a select few.&#13; Duane and his colleague Teresa Gomez were hunting for the rare candidates with the right stuff. With between 2,400 and 3,100 applications for only 11 to 35 spots, they had to quickly size up who had potential and who didn‚Äôt. From what they could see, Jos√© Hernandez didn‚Äôt have it.&#13; NASA had no idea that Jos√© was raised in poverty by undocumented immigrants. To make ends meet, the entire family took a long road trip from central Mexico to Northern California each winter. They stopped at farms along the way to pick everything from strawberries and grapes to tomatoes and cucumbers. Come fall, they headed back down to Mexico for a few months and then started the routine again. The journey forced Jos√© to miss several months of school and scrape by during the rest of the year in three different districts. After Jos√© started second grade, his father began cobbling together day jobs so they could stay in one place, but Jos√© still had to work weekends in the fields to help support his family. That left him with limited time for homework, and he couldn‚Äôt rely on his parents for assistance‚Äîthey only had third-grade educations.&#13; That history was invisible to NASA. In their search for the right stuff, they didn‚Äôt have access to the right stuff. ‚ÄúWhat should figure into the process is how hard it was for them to get there,‚Äù Duane Ross told me recently, having retired after half a century at NASA. ‚ÄúEarly on, we developed our own application forms so we could ask about that. Then the government decided that all applications had to be exactly the same, so we lost a lot of that.‚Äù With thousands of candidates, they could only check references on 400 and interview the top 120.&#13; For the initial screen, the federally regulated application process focused on work experience, education, special skills, and honors and awards. The form didn‚Äôt ask for unconventional skills like picking grapes. It didn‚Äôt signal that gaining a command of the English language would qualify as an honor. The awards section wasn‚Äôt a place to mention passing physics while working in the fields. The system wasn‚Äôt designed to identify and weigh the adversity candidates had overcome.&#13; This reinforced Jos√©‚Äôs belief that his background should stay underground. The last section of the application asked for other experience in pertinent activities, like flying. When I asked Jos√© why he didn‚Äôt volunteer his history as a migrant farmworker, he said, ‚ÄúI didn‚Äôt think it had any relevance. I even thought it might hurt me in a world where I was trying to assimilate as a professional.‚Äù If NASA had been aware of his past difficulties, they might have caught a glimpse of his future potential.&#13; Quantifying the Unquantifiable&#13; We all know that performance depends on more than ability‚Äîit‚Äôs also a function of degree of difficulty. How capable you appear to be is often a reflection of how hard your task is. The same Jeopardy! contestant will look smarter on the $200 questions than the $1,000 stumpers. The same comedian will seem funnier in front of a tipsy crowd at a nightclub than with a bunch of bankers in the morning.&#13; Yet when we judge potential, we often focus on execution and ignore degree of difficulty. We inadvertently favor candidates who aced easy tasks and dismiss those who passed taxing trials. We don‚Äôt see the skills they‚Äôve developed to overcome obstacles‚Äîespecially the skills that don‚Äôt show up on a resume.&#13; Many systems aren‚Äôt designed to surface and measure degree of difficulty . . . because doing that is, well, difficult. Some have tried‚Äîand failed miserably. In 2019, the SAT introduced an adversity score, awarding students up to 100 points for the hardships they faced in their families, neighborhoods, and schools. The backlash was so fervent that it didn‚Äôt even last the year. There was little consensus on the types of adversity to count, let alone how to score them.&#13; Social scientists have long found that people can have dramatically different reactions to the same event. One person‚Äôs trauma may be another‚Äôs setback; one person‚Äôs roadblock is another‚Äôs hurdle. We can calculate the degree of difficulty in a dive, but there isn‚Äôt a formula to quantify the degree of difficulty in a life.&#13; This is a problem that has long plagued affirmative action efforts. Creating policies that favor underrepresented groups is a politically charged issue. Liberals and conservatives have heated debates about whether it levels the playing field by compensating for historical injustice or perpetuates injustice by introducing reverse discrimination. Wherever you stand ideologically, as a social scientist, it‚Äôs my job to look to the best evidence. It turns out that affirmative action is often a double-edged sword‚Äîeven for the people it‚Äôs designed to serve.&#13; In a meta-analysis of 45 studies, when their organizations had affirmative action, members of disadvantaged groups struggled more with their tasks and got poorer performance reviews. The mere presence of an affirmative action policy was enough to raise questions about their competence in the eyes of observers (did they really deserve that promotion?) and in their own minds (did I get in on my own merit?). This effect even held in experiments establishing that women and racial minorities were highly qualified.&#13; Many groups are still restrained by cultural and structural shackles. It‚Äôs important to find systematic ways to open doors for people who have been deprived of opportunity. But it‚Äôs unfortunate that these well-meaning efforts are implemented in ways that leave the intended beneficiaries‚Äîand others‚Äîwondering whether they earned their place. Even if we could solve that problem, policies that address group hardship don‚Äôt capture all the difficulties individuals have endured.&#13; When professional orchestras finally started making concerted efforts to hire women, a popular solution was to have candidates audition from behind a screen. Being unable to identify the gender of musicians forced evaluators to focus on their skill. Although that improved the odds for women, it didn‚Äôt entirely close the gender gap. Since women didn‚Äôt have access to the same professional training, affirmative action advocates might argue for gender quotas‚Äîor for temporarily lowering skill requirements for women based on the disadvantages they‚Äôd faced as a group. But doing so runs the risk of casting doubt on the competence of female musicians. Accounting for their individual degree of difficulty points to a more helpful solution: adjusting skill expectations by access to opportunity. For example, orchestra auditions would have different standards for candidates who were self-taught than those who trained at Juilliard.&#13; The goal of measuring degree of difficulty at the individual level isn‚Äôt to advantage people who face adversity. It‚Äôs to make sure we don‚Äôt disadvantage people for navigating adversity. It seems that personal essays would give us a window into college applicants‚Äô challenges, but students who have experienced extreme suffering are understandably distraught at the thought of advertising their trauma and marketing their pain. Meanwhile, those who have been lucky enough to avoid significant setbacks often feel pressure to exaggerate their own struggles. Ultimately, the key indicator of potential isn‚Äôt the severity of adversity people encounter‚Äîit‚Äôs how they react to it. That‚Äôs what a better selection system would assess.&#13; Making the Invisible Visible&#13; Too often, our selection systems fail to weigh achievements in the context of degree of difficulty. Research shows that when students apply to graduate school, admissions officers pay surprisingly little attention to the difficulty of their courses and majors. Acing easy classes might give you higher odds of acceptance than doing reasonably well in hard classes.&#13; Think about how unfair that is. If admissions officers were Olympic figure skating judges, they might have a skater who gets 6s on a quadruple axel losing to one who gets 8s on a simple loop. If they were choosing a financial advisor, they‚Äôd pick one who earned great returns in a bull market over one who achieved good returns in a bear market.&#13; I don‚Äôt blame admissions officers‚Äîor hiring managers. Many don‚Äôt know their proxy measures are poor, and few have been trained to look for better signs of potential. I‚Äôve been serving on Ivy League admissions committees and making hiring decisions for two decades, and it didn‚Äôt occur to me until now to look at applicants‚Äô grades relative to the difficulty of their majors. Without being able to compare one curriculum to another, I was ill-equipped to compare one applicant‚Äôs accomplishments to another‚Äôs. I should‚Äôve known better.&#13; Selection systems need to put performance in context. It‚Äôs like having wrestlers compete in their own weight class. A promising approach is to create metrics that objectively compare students to their peer group. Along with each student‚Äôs grades, transcripts should display the grade point averages and ranges for their schools and majors.&#13; Displaying the difficulty of the task is only one way of contextualizing performance. We can also adjust for difficulty outside the classroom by comparing students to peers in similar circumstances. Some schools have taken the promising step of expanding transcripts to show students‚Äô grades relative to their neighborhood. Experiments show that this can help admissions officers spot the potential in lower-income students without reducing their enthusiasm about students from families with greater means. In the United Kingdom, universities and employers are starting to weigh discrete signs of economic hardship‚Äîlike holding a work-study job and receiving free meals. I asked Duane Ross about this idea, and he told me that if this kind of information had appeared on Jos√©‚Äôs application, NASA would have given him a closer look. ‚ÄúIf a candidate was a migrant farmer, we‚Äôd better notice that‚Äîparticularly if he went on and did something so positive.‚Äù&#13; Although this approach can help us identify some uncut gems, many difficulties are more subjective and harder to measure than tough grading and financial hardship. We need a way to assess the distance people have traveled to overcome the unique obstacles on their path. The good news is that schools and employers already have access to some valuable data‚Äîif only they knew where to look.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Rise over Run&#13; In a startling study, economist George Bulman analyzed a massive dataset containing every high school graduate in Florida from 1999 to 2002. The goal was to investigate whether their grades would predict their future success‚Äîmeasured in terms of college graduation rates and income earned a decade later.&#13; Freshman year grades revealed nothing about students‚Äô potential for future success. Sophomore and junior year grades did matter‚Äîevery GPA point higher was worth 5 percent more income later. And senior year grades were twice as important: every GPA point was worth 10 percent higher income.&#13; But what really foreshadowed earning potential was whether students improved over time. Unfortunately, colleges typically erased that trajectory by collapsing it into a single score. They sorted students based on their average grades over four years, neglecting to consider whether they got better or worse.&#13; Similar patterns held for the odds of finishing college. Students whose grades improved from freshman to junior year of high school were significantly more likely to graduate from college‚Äîand less likely to drop out‚Äîthan those whose grades declined over the same period. But admissions officers didn‚Äôt take that delta into account.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; It‚Äôs hard to overstate how ridiculous that is. Schools judge you as much for your performance three years ago as for three months ago‚Äîand they don‚Äôt even bother to look at the most recent and relevant data at all. We penalize people who rise after rocky starts when we should be rewarding them for the distance they‚Äôve traveled.&#13; It‚Äôs time for universities and employers to add another metric. Along with GPA, I think they should be assessing GPT: grade point trajectory. They can calculate the rate of improvement over time with basic division: rise over run. Early failure followed by later success is a mark of hidden potential.&#13; On the basis of GPA alone, compared to the other engineers applying to become astronauts, Jos√© didn‚Äôt stand out. In college, he had gotten Cs in chemistry, calculus, and programming. His lackluster performance raised questions about whether he had the technical aptitude required to be a flight engineer or mission specialist. To their credit, NASA didn‚Äôt have strict grade cutoffs. But they did favor candidates who excelled academically. And they didn‚Äôt know why Jos√©‚Äôs grades had suffered‚Äîor why they improved over time.&#13; To afford tuition, Jos√© worked the graveyard shift at a fruit and vegetable cannery, arriving at 10 p.m. and finishing at 6 a.m. It was a strain to stay alert in class, let alone master the material. When the fruit season ended, he worked nights and weekends as a restaurant busboy. Between demanding classes and a grueling schedule, he finished his first semester with a C average.&#13; As he struggled academically, Jos√© started to feel like an outsider and question his abilities. Extensive evidence has identified a social class achievement gap: first-generation college students tend to underperform academically due to a series of invisible disadvantages. The expectation to pave their own paths discourages them from seeking help. The pressure to pay their own way, the presence of self-doubt, and the absence of belonging all interfere with their ability to focus.&#13; The first semester of college was a particularly bumpy period for Jos√©. Things improved as he found work with more reasonable hours, established a more sustainable routine, and took the initiative to seek tutoring to fill the gaps in his knowledge. With each semester, his grades climbed. His GPA rose from 2.41 to 2.9 from his freshman fall to spring, and then up again to 3.33 and 3.56 for the fall and spring of his sophomore year. He went on to earn many As and graduate with cum laude honors. He won a full scholarship to a master‚Äôs program in engineering at the University of California, Santa Barbara. Although he didn‚Äôt have a perfect GPA, he aced grade point trajectory.&#13; One caveat when using improvement as a mark of potential: it‚Äôs important to set reasonable expectations. In an initial screen, upward trajectories are hints that candidates have overcome adversity. But we can‚Äôt always expect a sharp rise. When people face major setbacks, the slopes they have to climb get steeper, and maintaining steady performance can be an achievement in itself.&#13; No single measure of improvement should be our sole metric. Trajectories are a valuable start, but they don‚Äôt paint a full picture of potential. To gauge the distance people are capable of traveling on steep slopes, it‚Äôs also critical to take a closer look at the skills and abilities they‚Äôve gained so far. Instead of looking at past experience or past performance, we should find out what they‚Äôve learned and how well they can learn. And to do that, we need to rethink how we interview people. The most fascinating approach I‚Äôve seen is at a call center in Israel.&#13; Interview without a Vampire&#13; Several decades ago, a therapist in training named Gil Winch became frustrated with clinical psychology. It wasn‚Äôt enough to help one client at a time‚Äîhe wanted to solve problems on a larger scale. One day, when talking with a neighbor who was paralyzed, Gil learned that worldwide, people with disabilities were struggling to find work. People with impairments in their hearing, vision, movement, memory, learning, and communication shared a common experience. Whether they had a physical disability or a psychological disorder, they knew from a lifetime of stigma and rejection that they were likely to be underestimated and overlooked.&#13; Gil noticed that job interviews put people with disabilities at a disadvantage. A typical interview is set up like an interrogation. Evaluators grill you on your shortcomings: What are your greatest weaknesses? Here‚Äôs a list of every mistake I‚Äôve ever made, in order. They ask you impossible questions about your future: Where do you see yourself in five years? Taking your job and asking better interview questions. Some even try to stump you with brainteasers: How many golf balls can you fit in a jumbo jet? Why would anyone fill a jet with golf balls? [*] Even for candidates who aren‚Äôt facing a disability, this approach amplifies anxiety and awkwardness.&#13; The stress created in interviews prevents us from seeing people‚Äôs full potential. That stress tends to be especially pronounced for people who have been underestimated in the past. Just knowing that there‚Äôs a stereotype about your group is enough to undermine your performance under pressure. The fear of confirming negative stereotypes has been shown to disrupt focus and drain working memory, obscuring the abilities of women on math tests, immigrants on verbal tests, Black students on the SAT, older adults on cognitive tests, and students with physical and learning disabilities on a range of tests. They‚Äôre set up to fail.&#13; Gil wanted to showcase the abilities of people with disabilities. To make sure their differences didn‚Äôt stop them from traveling great distances, he did something radical: he launched a call center staffed entirely by people with disabilities. He named it Call Yachol, which is Hebrew for ‚Äúable to do anything.‚Äù To set job candidates up for success, he reversed the standard interview process. The system he created is filled with surprises.&#13; Before you show up, you fill out a questionnaire about your passions‚Äîfrom cherished books to beloved music to favorite hobbies. For support, you‚Äôre invited to bring a partner or a pet to the interview. As soon as you arrive, you discover that the interviewers are the opposite of interrogators: they‚Äôre hosts. They give you a tour, offer you coffee or tea, and treat you like a guest in their home. They direct you to what looks like a living room, with big, comfy chairs, and ask you about some of your passions. The goal isn‚Äôt just to help you relax; it‚Äôs also a chance for them to see you light up about what you love.&#13; Next, you get to showcase your strengths. Instead of bombarding you with intimidating riddles and unfamiliar problems, Gil handcrafted a series of challenges that give you the opportunity to exhibit your skills in familiar situations. Keen to display your determination in the face of obstacles? Get ready to meet a difficult neighbor who objects to all your ideas for renovating your building. Excited to display your attention to detail? It‚Äôs time for Don‚Äôt Kill Granny. She‚Äôs allergic to peanuts, and your task is to pick out the safe items for her from a long grocery list. Want to prove you excel at persuasion and negotiation? Tell us how you‚Äôd convince a teenager not to look at his phone during dinner.&#13; In the science of interviewing, there‚Äôs a name for these kinds of demonstrations. They‚Äôre called work samples. A work sample is a snapshot of an applicant‚Äôs skills. Sometimes you can provide one by submitting a portfolio of your past work. Many colleges have those built into their admissions processes, inviting students to submit their creative portfolios. You can send in recordings if you‚Äôre a musician, scripts if you‚Äôre a screenwriter or playwright, and videos if you‚Äôre an actor, dancer, or magician.&#13; But past work samples have similar limitations to past performance. They leave us comparing apples and oranges‚Äîwith no way to account for the different difficulties candidates have endured to date. A powerful alternative is to create real-time work samples: give everyone the same problem to solve in the present. There‚Äôs a wealth of evidence that these kinds of live work samples can fill gaps in interviews by illuminating candidates‚Äô capabilities. Instead of relying solely on what people say, you get to observe what they can do‚Äîwhich applicants appreciate.&#13; I first stumbled onto work samples early in my career, when I didn‚Äôt know they had a name. A colleague and I were hiring a team of salespeople, and we decided to ask them to sell us a rotten apple. One applicant‚Äôs pitch was unforgettable: ‚ÄúThis may look like a rotten apple, but it‚Äôs actually an aged, antique apple. They say an apple a day keeps the doctor away, and the longer they age, the more nutrients they gain! And you can plant the seeds in your backyard.‚Äù After resolving some honesty concerns, that particular candidate ended up being the best salesperson I ever hired. Since then, I‚Äôve seen a range of creative approaches to real-time work samples across industries. My favorites include schools that evaluate the proactivity of teaching applicants by asking them to prepare a live class, and manufacturing firms that assess the prosocial skills of aircraft mechanic candidates by asking them to build a Lego helicopter together.&#13; Work samples are often one-and-done. But your first effort is rarely your best effort. That‚Äôs another stumbling block the system at Call Yachol removes. Their work samples are designed to give people second chances to succeed. If you get stuck when you‚Äôre trying not to kill Granny, you can call a timeout and ask for some help. At the end of the interview, instead of being judged, you get to be the judge. You‚Äôre asked to rate your interview experience‚Äîhow welcome the interviewer made you feel, and whether you performed at your best. If you‚Äôre not happy with how it went, you get a do-over. They ask you what they can do differently to get to know you better.&#13; When a man named Harvey showed up at Call Yachol for his second interview, it was clear that he was struggling to concentrate. The interviewer paused and asked him how he was feeling. Harvey was on the autism spectrum, and he explained that when his shoes felt uncomfortable that morning, he spilled coffee on his shirt while trying to fix them and missed his bus. He was flustered after scrambling to arrive on time, and his shoes still didn‚Äôt feel right. The interviewer called a break and gave him an hour to reset. Harvey aced the redo and got the job.&#13; Collecting work samples takes time. But many work samples can now be gathered online‚Äîit‚Äôs easier than ever to create digital problem-solving tasks. Even if we run them in person, it‚Äôs no more time-consuming than conducting an interview. We invest that time because we know how much it matters to be thoughtful about who we let in the door. And despite all the developments in artificial intelligence, I have yet to meet an algorithm that would spot the potential in Harvey.[*] He has a difficult cold-calling job, where rudeness and rejection are the norm. Most people quit in the first few years, but Harvey has been a paragon of grit and resilience. He‚Äôs been a star for eight years, consistently reaching his monthly goals and receiving an award in front of the whole call center as the employee of the quarter.&#13; Industry insiders were skeptical that Gil‚Äôs hiring model would work‚Äîespecially for a call center. They didn‚Äôt expect people with disabilities to thrive in a fast-paced, high-pressure environment. It took Gil a full year to recruit his first client. When it finally happened in 2009, he hired 15 people with a range of disabilities and disorders. In one case, he had a manager who was legally blind supervising an employee with hearing loss. It didn‚Äôt sound like a recipe for success, but Gil was confident it would work. Having seen their strengths up close, he knew the distance the team was capable of traveling. They didn‚Äôt just meet expectations‚Äîthey shattered them. Since then, as Call Yachol has grown, many of their teams have exceeded industry benchmarks for hourly leads and time on the phone with clients, and some have outperformed teams without disabilities.&#13; To Gil, that was only the beginning. He knew that people with disabilities were only one group of people whose potential had been ignored. He expanded his hiring model to create opportunities for other disadvantaged groups‚Äîfrom immigrants to the formerly incarcerated. In 2018, the team was invited to Israel‚Äôs parliament to receive an award honoring the good they‚Äôd done for individuals and society.&#13; An interview model like Call Yachol‚Äôs is not just a compelling way to open the door to underdogs. I think it‚Äôs a way to recognize the potential in everyone‚Äîit enables each candidate‚Äôs skills to shine through. Interrogations make all of us anxious, and anyone can run into mishaps on the way to a job interview. Skills are best gauged by what people can do, not what they say or what they‚Äôve done before. Instead of trying to trip people up, we should give them the chance to put their best foot forward. How they respond in a do-over is a more meaningful window into their character than how they handle the first try.&#13; A Window of Opportunity&#13; Since NASA invited candidates to update their applications every year, Jos√© got an annual do-over. By 1996, after a string of rejections, he was on the verge of quitting when his wife, Adela, encouraged him not to throw away his dream. ‚ÄúLet NASA be the one to disqualify you,‚Äù she urged. ‚ÄúDon‚Äôt disqualify yourself.‚Äù&#13; Jos√© realized there was more he could do to qualify himself: he would ‚Äúbecome a sponge.‚Äù He learned that most astronauts were pilots and scuba divers, so he took a year to earn his pilot‚Äôs license and spent another year driving to scuba diving training every weekend until he got his basic, advanced, and master certifications. And when his federal lab presented him with an unconventional opportunity to work on curtailing nuclear proliferation in Siberia, Jos√© took it on one condition: he would get to learn to speak Russian as part of the deal. He hoped it would help him stand out in NASA‚Äôs next cycle.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; In 1998, when Jos√© was 36, he sent off another astronaut application. And at long last, there was encouraging news. Of over 2,500 applicants, he was one of 120 finalists.&#13; Jos√© finally had the opportunity to provide a complete, live work sample. He went through a full week of physical and psychological assessment at Johnson Space Center. Former astronauts queried him on engineering and flying technicalities and teamwork and communication skills. He took tests that required him to rotate objects in his mind and solve problems under pressure. Out of a possible 99 points, the astronaut selection board gave Jos√© a score of 91.&#13; The interviewers didn‚Äôt ask him about hardship directly. They gave him an hour to talk about his background. For the first time, feeling confident that he had proven his technical skills, Jos√© opened up and told NASA he‚Äôd started out as a migrant farmer. ‚ÄúIf you could accomplish all that by coming from someplace like Jos√© did,‚Äù Duane Ross says, ‚Äúto overcome all that and get to the same place other people got, then you have a lot of desire and capability.‚Äù&#13; After the interview, Jos√© got a personal call from Duane. Unfortunately, they were rejecting him again. But this time, there was a silver lining. They were offering him a job . . . not as an astronaut, but as an engineer.&#13; Jos√© had been adapting every year. Now he would have to adapt again. Although he might not be going up himself, he could be part of the mission to send humans to space. The experience taught him a lesson: ‚ÄúThere is more than one star in the sky and more than one goal and purpose in life.‚Äù&#13; &#13; ‚Äî&#13; After a number of years working as a NASA engineer, in 2004, Jos√© heard his phone ring. The voice on the other end of the line asked if he was replaceable. Jos√© said he was happy to train someone to take his place. ‚ÄúGood,‚Äù the manager said. ‚ÄúHow would you like to come work for the astronaut office?‚Äù&#13; After 15 years of applying, Jos√© was selected to go to space. ‚ÄúThe second I heard the good news,‚Äù he recalls, ‚Äúmy whole body went numb.‚Äù He raced home to break the news to his wife, children, and parents, who celebrated by hugging and dancing.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; In August 2009, a few weeks after turning 47, Jos√© stepped into the space shuttle. He sat down, buckled in, and braced himself for takeoff. Just before midnight, he heard the countdown and watched the engines light up. Eight and a half minutes after blasting into the sky, the engine shut off, and Jos√© couldn‚Äôt believe his eyes. To convince himself it was real, he tossed a piece of equipment up. Watching it hover, he marveled, ‚ÄúI guess we are in space!‚Äù&#13; Jos√© had gone from picking strawberries in fields to floating among the stars. Over the course of two weeks in space, he flew over five million miles. It was a short hop compared to the distance he had traveled for the chance to wear a space suit.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; As exciting as it is to see a candidate like Jos√© succeed, it isn‚Äôt enough. His success shows us what we‚Äôre missing in so many others. He had to break the mold to make it through a broken system. He‚Äôs the exception, but he should be the rule.&#13; When we evaluate people, there‚Äôs nothing more rewarding than finding a diamond in the rough. Our job isn‚Äôt to apply the pressure that brings out their brilliance. It‚Äôs to make sure we don‚Äôt overlook those who have already faced that pressure‚Äîand recognize their potential to shine.&#13; &#13;

 &#13; Epilogue&#13; Going the Distance&#13; &#13; Hold fast to dreams&#13; For if dreams die&#13; Life is a broken-winged bird&#13; That cannot fly.&#13; ‚ÄîLangston Hughes&#13; &#13; When I started telling people I was writing a book on potential, I kept getting questions about dreams. Is it about reaching our dreams? Are you going to encourage readers to dream big? I didn‚Äôt want to go there. It sounded too Pollyanna, too juvenile. It‚Äôs the sort of lofty language peddled by self-help gurus, not studied by serious social scientists.&#13; If you have the vision, gravitas, and moral high ground of Martin Luther King Jr., you might get away with it. Though even King‚Äôs advisors feared the dream language would sound ‚Äúhackneyed and trite.‚Äù For the rest of us, I figured dreams were best left behind in childhood.&#13; Then I came across new evidence that people with bigger dreams go on to achieve greater things. When economists tracked thousands of people from birth until age 55, the aspirations they formed as adolescents foreshadowed how their adult lives would unfold. Young people with grander dreams went further in school and climbed higher at work. Even after accounting for a host of other factors‚Äîtheir cognitive skills, character skills, family income, and parents‚Äô education, occupations, and aspirations‚Äîtheir own dreams made a unique contribution to how they progressed and who they became.&#13; I realized that if it weren‚Äôt for my dreams and the people who helped make them a reality, this book wouldn‚Äôt exist.&#13; &#13; ‚Äî&#13; At the start of my senior year of high school, I was planning to stay in Michigan for college. But one night in September, I went to sleep and dreamed that I went to Harvard. It wasn‚Äôt an option I‚Äôd ever entertained before. I knew it was a long shot‚ÄîI wasn‚Äôt sure if I was qualified, and my family couldn‚Äôt afford out-of-state tuition. But having recently seen Good Will Hunting, I couldn‚Äôt get the idea out of my head.&#13; In my hometown in the suburbs of Detroit, people earned status based on shallow qualities like wealth and beauty. The popular kids at my school were mostly rich or hot. In my vision of Cambridge, it was cool to be smart. My dorm would be filled with fellow nerds. My classes would allow me to soak up knowledge from the world‚Äôs brightest minds.&#13; Later that fall, I put on my only suit for an interview with a Harvard alum at a nearby law office. Just as I was leaving home, a thought crossed my mind. I ran back inside, grabbed a small box from a drawer, and stuck it in my jacket pocket.&#13; When I arrived, I was shaking‚ÄîI‚Äôd never been interviewed before, and I‚Äôd never met a Harvard grad either. What if I didn‚Äôt understand a word he said?&#13; The interview was scheduled for an hour, but it lasted for three. I had no clue what to make of that. Every day afterward, when I got off the bus after school, I checked the mailbox nervously. In December, I found an envelope from Harvard.&#13; It was an acceptance letter, and it came with some financial aid‚Äîthough I would still have to work my way through school. I was so overjoyed that I broke my no-dancing rule and did a touchdown dance. Only later did I learn that whenever I did something stupid, I would forever hear people say, ‚ÄúYOU went to Harvard?!‚Äù&#13; In the spring, as I met my future classmates at events and online, I saw a pattern. Harvard seemed to attract two extremes of students: those who were sure they were a gift to the world, and those who feared they were the one mistake. I was in the latter camp. I‚Äôd somehow made the cut, but I didn‚Äôt know if I was smart enough to cut it there.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; In my first week on campus, I would get the chance to find out if I was smart enough.&#13; Before classes started, every new student had to take a mandatory writing exam. It would determine our placement in a freshman writing seminar. If we passed, we‚Äôd have a semester of writing and be done. If we failed, we‚Äôd have to start the year with an extra semester of remedial writing. A sophomore told me not to worry about it‚Äîremedial writing was for jocks and international students who spoke English as a fifth language.&#13; I don‚Äôt remember the writing prompt, but I remember what I wrote: an analysis of the characters in Good Will Hunting. A few days later, the envelope arrived under my door. I had failed the writing test.&#13; I did not like them apples.&#13; It was the first time Harvard was judging my intellect, and the jury came back with a devastating verdict. On the charge of not being smart enough: guilty. Forget impostor syndrome; I was an actual fraud. Meanwhile, my roommate‚Äîa heavily recruited quarterback on the football team‚Äîpassed.&#13; To find out what my options were, I set up a meeting with the experts at the writing center who graded my exam. They said that ultimately, it was up to students to decide which class to take, but they were strongly advising me to take the remedial class. After failing the test, when students went straight into the regular class, they‚Äôd never gotten better than a B minus. The writing experts confided that from reading my essay, it would be even riskier for me. My writing was so unclear and unstructured that if I skipped the remedial class, I‚Äôd probably get a C. But it was up to me.&#13; I was torn. On the one hand, I didn‚Äôt want to ruin my GPA, and I enjoyed writing and wanted to get better at it. On the other hand, I was embarrassed at the thought of being held back, and I didn‚Äôt want to waste an elective.&#13; To make the decision, I needed a guide. But the people who knew me best didn‚Äôt know Harvard . . . and the people at Harvard didn‚Äôt really know me at all. Then I thought of my alumni interviewer, a lawyer named John Gierak. He‚Äôd been involved in Harvard admissions for decades, and he‚Äôd invested a lot of time getting to know me.&#13; A few months earlier, I‚Äôd seen John at a welcome reception for admitted students and asked him why I got in. He responded that he wasn‚Äôt on the committee, so he didn‚Äôt know for sure. But there was something he‚Äôd highlighted in his interview report to Harvard that wasn‚Äôt visible in my application.&#13; &#13; ‚Äî&#13; The previous fall, when I arrived at his law office for my interview, John opened by asking me about my interests and hobbies. ‚ÄúI see you‚Äôve performed as a magician,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúWhat‚Äôs your favorite trick?‚Äù&#13; I reached into my pocket and pulled out the small box I‚Äôd grabbed on my way out the door: a deck of cards. ‚ÄúActually, can I show you?‚Äù&#13; John smiled. I started shuffling the cards and told a story with the entire deck. Each time I named a card, it would magically appear at the top‚Äîeven after I let him cut the pack.&#13; At the end, audiences usually asked how I did it. But John wanted to know how I‚Äôd learned it. I told him I‚Äôd seen a magician do it on TV when I was twelve and figured out a way to do my own version.&#13; John asked if I could perform with someone else‚Äôs cards. He went hunting through his law office and came back a few minutes later with a deck. I did a few more tricks‚Äîsome that I found in books, and others that I‚Äôd made up myself.&#13; At the welcome reception, John told me it wasn‚Äôt the magic that helped me stand out. It was the initiative I‚Äôd taken in teaching myself‚Äîand the courage I‚Äôd shown in doing an impromptu performance for him. It was my first interview‚ÄîI didn‚Äôt know we were just supposed to talk, and it was only after becoming an organizational psychologist that I realized I‚Äôd given him a work sample.&#13; By sharing what stood out in my interview, John had given me a crash course in the importance of character skills. My success wouldn‚Äôt depend on my initial ability. It would depend on my ability and motivation to learn.&#13; When I failed Harvard‚Äôs writing test, they hadn‚Äôt declared me a failure as a writer. They‚Äôd failed a tiny snapshot of my writing. They didn‚Äôt know me, so I set out to prove them wrong. I was determined to go from failing the test to acing the class.&#13; I skipped the remedial writing seminar and signed up for the regular one. I became a sponge, embracing the discomfort of seeking endless rounds of constructive criticism from the professor and anyone else who would read my writing. Instead of going home for Thanksgiving, I stayed on campus to write and rewrite and rewrite an essay. By the end of the semester, I‚Äôd traveled a great distance. The professor congratulated me on earning the only A in the class.&#13; I loved them apples.&#13; Impostor syndrome says, ‚ÄúI don‚Äôt know what I‚Äôm doing. It‚Äôs only a matter of time until everyone finds out.‚Äù&#13; Growth mindset says, ‚ÄúI don‚Äôt know what I‚Äôm doing yet. It‚Äôs only a matter of time until I figure it out.‚Äù&#13; Scaffolding gives you the support you need to figure it out.&#13; I haven‚Äôt seen John Gierak in two decades. But the insight he gave me about the power of character skills was a critical bit of scaffolding. It became my compass, and it guided my progress for years afterward.&#13; Looking back, if I hadn‚Äôt challenged myself to take the regular seminar, I might never have become a writer‚Äîor a psychologist. The writing seminars covered a range of topics, and for my seminar that fall I was able to pick social influence. One of the assigned readings happened to be a book by a psychologist, Robert Cialdini. It was filled with surprising evidence, and I found it so riveting that when it was assigned for one of my other classes, I read it a second time. It was the first time I considered becoming a psychologist. I started dreaming of writing a book like that one day.&#13; For the next decade, that dream faded into the background. As a professor, I had to figure out how to teach. Even after I got my anxiety under control, I was still fairly robotic until a mentor, Jane Dutton, dropped a pin. She advised me to unleash my inner magician.&#13; I started introducing surprise endings with counterintuitive studies and unexpected twists in experiential learning. I was focused on teaching memorable classes and doing worthwhile research. Then I got tenure at Wharton. Having made it to the top of that mountain, I wanted to do more to help others climb theirs. I felt a responsibility to share what I was learning beyond the classroom and academic journals‚Äîwith an audience broader than students and researchers.&#13; A few weeks later, a mentor reached out to tell me he was starting his next book. It was on incentives, and since I studied motivation, he wondered if I wanted to coauthor it with him. He was one of my role models, and I told my students how honored and excited I was that he was bringing me on board. They revolted. If you‚Äôre going to write a book, start with your own ideas! Have you forgotten what you taught us? The worst kind of success is achieving other people‚Äôs goals. Don‚Äôt live someone else‚Äôs dream. They were right. I decided to pursue a book of my own.&#13; Several colleagues raved about a literary agent, Richard Pine, who took me under his wing. In June, after many weeks of independent brainwriting, Richard announced that it was time to draft a proposal to share with potential publishers. I‚Äôd been studying the topic for years, so the ideas came pouring out. In August, I sent him 103,914 words. In two months, I‚Äôd written a draft of the whole book.&#13; I couldn‚Äôt wait to hear Richard‚Äôs reaction. Gently, he explained that it was too academic. That was an understatement‚Äîit was boring. In other words: not a page-turner. I was so deep in the weeds of the research that even my academic colleagues would lose interest. He encouraged me to reflect on the bigger picture and start over from scratch.&#13; Hello, languishing.&#13; I was stuck. I didn‚Äôt know where to begin, let alone if I could do it. Impostor syndrome came back with a vengeance. Who was I to write a book? Why would anyone want to read what I had to say? Richard shifted from judge to coach and told me to stop doubting myself. ‚ÄúOf course you can do it! Just write like you teach, not like you write for academic journals.‚Äù&#13; I trusted him. He wasn‚Äôt just a credible believer; he was a guide pointing me in a better direction. I threw out 102,000 words‚Äîthere were about four pages worth salvaging‚Äîand wrote what became my first book. Writing like I teach has been my compass ever since. It‚Äôs what guided me to write books that you were actually interested in reading. Well, at least one . . . I hope you don‚Äôt regret it.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13; Not long ago, it dawned on me that impostor syndrome is a paradox:&#13; &#13; &#13; Others believe in you&#13; &#13; &#13; You don‚Äôt believe in yourself&#13; &#13; &#13; Yet you believe yourself instead of them&#13; &#13; &#13; If you doubt yourself, shouldn‚Äôt you also doubt your low opinion of yourself?&#13; I now believe that impostor syndrome is a sign of hidden potential. It feels like other people are overestimating you, but it‚Äôs more likely that you‚Äôre underestimating yourself. They‚Äôve recognized a capacity for growth that you can‚Äôt see yet. When multiple people believe in you, it might be time to believe them.&#13; Many people dream of achieving goals. They measure their progress by the status they acquire and the accolades they collect. But the gains that count the most are the hardest to count. The most meaningful growth is not building our careers‚Äîit‚Äôs building our character.&#13; Success is more than reaching our goals‚Äîit‚Äôs living our values. There‚Äôs no higher value than aspiring to be better tomorrow than we are today. There‚Äôs no greater accomplishment than unleashing our hidden potential.&#13; &#13;

 &#13; Actions for Impact&#13; To take a quiz about your hidden potential, visit www.adamgrant.net.&#13; The learning process isn‚Äôt finished when we acquire knowledge. It‚Äôs complete when we consistently apply that knowledge. Here are my top forty practical takeaways for unlocking hidden potential and achieving greater things.&#13; I. BUILD CHARACTER SKILLS&#13; &#13; &#13; Unleash hidden potential through character skills. The people who grow the most aren‚Äôt the smartest people in the room. They‚Äôre the ones who strive to make themselves and others smarter. When opportunity doesn‚Äôt knock, look for ways to build a door‚Äîor climb through a window.&#13; &#13; &#13; A. Become a creature of discomfort&#13; &#13; &#13; Don‚Äôt be afraid to try a new style. Instead of focusing on the way you like to learn, embrace the discomfort of matching the method to the task. Reading and writing are usually best for critical thinking. Listening is ideal for understanding emotions, and doing is better for remembering information.&#13; &#13; &#13; Use it or never gain it at all. Put yourself in the ring before you feel ready. You don‚Äôt need to get comfortable before you can practice your skills‚Äîyour comfort grows as you practice your skills. As polyglots show us, even experts have to start from day one.&#13; &#13; &#13; Seek discomfort. Instead of just striving to learn, aim to feel uncomfortable. Pursuing discomfort sets you on a faster path to growth. If you want to get it right, it has to first feel wrong.&#13; &#13; &#13; Set a mistake budget. To encourage trial and error, set a goal for the minimum number of mistakes you want to make per day or per week. When you expect to stumble, you ruminate about it less‚Äîand improve more.&#13; &#13; &#13; B. Become a sponge&#13; &#13; &#13; Increase your absorptive capacity. Seek out new knowledge, skills, and perspectives to fuel your growth‚Äînot feed your ego. Progress hinges on the quality of the information you take in, not on the quantity of information you seek out.&#13; &#13; &#13; Ask for advice, not feedback. Feedback is backward-looking‚Äîit leads people to criticize you or cheer for you. Advice is forward-looking‚Äîit leads people to coach you. You can get your critics and cheerleaders to act more like coaches by asking a simple question: ‚ÄúWhat‚Äôs one thing I can do better next time?‚Äù&#13; &#13; &#13; Figure out which sources to trust. Decide what information is worth absorbing‚Äîand which should be filtered out. Listen to the coaches who have relevant expertise (credibility), know you well (familiarity), and want what‚Äôs best for you (care).&#13; &#13; &#13; Be the coach you hope to have. Demonstrate that honesty is the highest expression of loyalty. Model effective coaching by being forthcoming in what you say and respectful in how you say it. Show people how easy it is to hear a hard truth from someone who believes in their potential and cares about their success.&#13; &#13; &#13; C. Become an imperfectionist&#13; &#13; &#13; Strive for excellence, not perfection. Progress comes from maintaining high standards, not eliminating every flaw. Practice wabi sabi, the art of honoring beauty in imperfection, by identifying some shortcomings that you can accept. Consider where you truly need the best and where you can settle for good enough. Mark your growth with Eric Best‚Äôs questions: Did you make yourself better today? Did you make someone else better today?&#13; &#13; &#13; Enlist judges to gauge your progress. To figure out whether you‚Äôve created a minimum lovable product, ask a few people to independently rate your work on a scale of 0 to 10. Whatever score you receive, ask them how you can get closer to 10. Be sure to set an acceptable as well as aspirational result‚Äîand don‚Äôt forget that to get high scores on your top priorities, you may have to be satisfied with lower scores on the others.&#13; &#13; &#13; Be your own last judge. It‚Äôs better to disappoint others than to disappoint yourself. Before you release something into the world, assess whether it represents you well. If this was the only work people saw of yours, would you be proud of it?&#13; &#13; &#13; Engage in mental time travel. When you‚Äôre struggling to appreciate your progress, consider how your past self would view your current achievements. If you knew five years ago what you‚Äôd accomplish now, how proud would you have been?&#13; &#13; &#13; II. SET UP SCAFFOLDING TO OVERCOME OBSTACLES&#13; &#13; &#13; Look outward for the right support at the right time. Every challenge requires its own support. The support you need isn‚Äôt permanent‚Äîit‚Äôs a temporary structure that gives you a foothold or a lift so you can keep climbing on your own.&#13; &#13; &#13; A. Turn practice into play&#13; &#13; &#13; Turn the daily grind into a source of daily joy. To maintain harmonious passion, design practice around deliberate play. Set up fun skill-building challenges‚Äîlike Evelyn Glennie learning to play a Bach piece on a snare drum, Steph Curry trying to score twenty-one points in a minute, or medical residents honing their nonverbal communication skills by using nonsense words in improv comedy games.&#13; &#13; &#13; Compete against yourself. Measure your progress over time, not against an opponent. The risk of competing against others is that you can win without getting better. When you compete against yourself, the only way to win is to grow.&#13; &#13; &#13; Don‚Äôt hold yourself hostage to a fixed routine. It‚Äôs possible to avoid burnout and boreout by introducing novelty and variety into your practice. You can alternate between different skills you‚Äôre practicing or switch up the tools and methods you use to learn those skills. Even small tweaks can make a big difference.&#13; &#13; &#13; Be proactive about rest and recovery. Don‚Äôt wait until you‚Äôre burned out or bored out to take breaks‚Äîbuild them into your schedule. Taking time off helps to sustain harmonious passion, unlock fresh ideas, and deepen learning. Relaxing is not a waste of time; it‚Äôs an investment in well-being.&#13; &#13; &#13; B. Take the roundabout path to progress&#13; &#13; &#13; When you‚Äôre stuck, back up to move forward. When you hit a dead end, it might be time to turn around and find a new path. It feels like regressing, but it‚Äôs often the only way to find a route to progress.&#13; &#13; &#13; Find a compass. You don‚Äôt need a map to start on a new route‚Äîyou just need a compass to gauge whether you‚Äôre heading in the right direction. A good compass is a credible source that signals when you‚Äôre going off course.&#13; &#13; &#13; Seek multiple guides. Instead of relying on a single expert or mentor, remember that the best directions come from multiple guides. Ask them about the landmarks and turning points from their own journeys‚Äîand tell them about the roads you‚Äôve taken so far. As they drop pins, you can piece them together into a route that works for you.&#13; &#13; &#13; Find a side gig. When you find yourself languishing, you can build momentum by taking a detour to a new destination. When you make progress in a side project or hobby, you rack up small wins, which remind you that forward movement is possible.&#13; &#13; &#13; C. Fly by your own bootstraps&#13; &#13; &#13; Teach what you want to learn. The best way to learn something is to teach it. You understand it better after you explain it‚Äîand you remember it better after you take the time to recall it. Like the Golden Thirteen, you can do this in groups, with each member teaching a distinct skill or slice of information.&#13; &#13; &#13; Build confidence by coaching others. When you‚Äôre doubting your ability to overcome an obstacle, instead of seeking advice, try giving advice. Guiding others through a challenge reminds you that you have the resources you need to tackle it. The advice you give is usually the advice you need to take.&#13; &#13; &#13; Harness both high and low expectations as motivation. If ignorant naysayers doubt you, take it as a challenge. Instead of letting them crush your confidence, recognize it as an opportunity to prove them wrong. And when credible believers are behind you, rise to the occasion and prove them right.&#13; &#13; &#13; Be a good ancestor. When your faith falters, recall who you‚Äôre fighting for. Our deepest reserves of resilience come from knowing that other people are counting on us.&#13; &#13; &#13; III. BUILD SYSTEMS OF OPPORTUNITY&#13; &#13; &#13; Open doors for people who are underrated and overlooked. Create systems that invest in and create opportunities for all‚Äînot just gifted students and high-potential employees. A good system gives underdogs and late bloomers the chance to show how far they‚Äôve come.&#13; &#13; &#13; A. Design schools to bring out the best in all students&#13; &#13; &#13; Don‚Äôt waste a brain. Recognize that intelligence comes in many forms, and every child has the potential to excel. Cultivate a growth mindset in teachers, not only in students. Gauge success by the progress of every student, not just those at the top.&#13; &#13; &#13; Professionalize education. Following Finland‚Äôs example, train and treat teachers as trusted professionals. When teachers are equipped and encouraged to stay up-to-date on the latest evidence, coach one another, and shape the curriculum, the next generation can achieve greater things.&#13; &#13; &#13; Keep students with the same teachers for multiple years. Looping allows teachers to specialize in their students, not just their subjects. With more time to get to know each student personally, teachers can become coaches and mentors, tailoring their instructional and emotional support to help all students reach their potential.&#13; &#13; &#13; Give students the freedom to explore and share their individual interests. The most important lesson to teach students is that learning is fun. When students get to select the activity stations, books, and projects that interest them, they‚Äôre more likely to develop intrinsic motivation. When they present on the topics they love, it reinforces their enthusiasm‚Äîand gives their classmates the chance to catch it.&#13; &#13; &#13; B. Unearth collective intelligence in teams&#13; &#13; &#13; Transform groups into teams. Collective intelligence depends on cohesion‚Äîaligning a team around shared responsibility for a meaningful mission. When people believe they need one another to succeed in reaching an important goal, they become more than the sum of their parts.&#13; &#13; &#13; Choose leaders based on prosocial skills. Instead of promoting babblers and ball hogs, elevate people who put the mission above their ego‚Äîand prioritize team cohesion over personal glory. When teams are eager to contribute, the most effective leader is not the loudest talker, but the best listener.&#13; &#13; &#13; Shift from brainstorming to brainwriting. For more balanced participation and better solutions, before you meet as a group, have people generate and evaluate ideas independently. Once all the ideas are on the table and all the voices are in the room, have the group select and refine the most promising possibilities.&#13; &#13; &#13; Replace the corporate ladder with a lattice system. Instead of leaving it up to a single boss to shoot down suggestions, give people multiple paths to speak up. If people can go to more than one leader, a single no can‚Äôt kill an idea‚Äîand a single yes can be enough to save it.&#13; &#13; &#13; C. Discover uncut gems in job interviews and college applications&#13; &#13; &#13; Eliminate requirements for credentials and experience. When evaluating others, beware of mistaking past accomplishments and experience for future potential. Background and talent determine where people start, but character skills shape how far they can climb.&#13; &#13; &#13; Account for degree of difficulty. Struggles don‚Äôt necessarily reflect the absence of ability‚Äîoften they reveal the presence of adversity. To account for the obstacles candidates have faced, put their performance in context by comparing them to peers in their school, major, and neighborhood.&#13; &#13; &#13; Use trajectories in evaluations. It‚Äôs not enough to look at recent or average performance‚Äîthe trajectory of performance over time matters more. An upward slope is a clue that candidates have overcome adversity.&#13; &#13; &#13; Reimagine interviews to set candidates up to succeed. Instead of designing interviews to maximize stress, create opportunities for candidates to shine. Invite applicants to share what they love and showcase their strengths. Afterward, ask if they thought their performance represented them well‚Äîand if not, give them a do-over.&#13; &#13; &#13; Redefine success. The most meaningful form of performance is progress. The ultimate mark of potential is not the height of the peak you‚Äôve reached, but the distance you‚Äôve traveled‚Äîand helped others travel.&#13; &#13; &#13; &#13;

 &#13; Acknowledgments&#13; This book would have fallen far short of its potential without the initiative of super-agent Richard Pine and crackerjack editor Rick Kot. Richard spurred me to think bigger and bolder from the first page to the last. Rick massaged drivel into prose and turned around drafts in record time. Together, the two of them guided me to turn around when I got stuck and made it a joy to explore new routes.&#13; Writing a book can be a lonely journey, but I had the great fortune of collaborating with the ultimate dynamic duo of judging and coaching. Marissa Shandell and Karren Knowlton immeasurably enriched every page of this book. Whenever they found a half-baked idea, they rapidly tore it down and expertly built it into something stronger. They poured their ingenuity into shaping each story, their intellect into sharpening each sentence, and their energy into elevating each illustration and takeaway. Marissa introduced solutions to problems I didn‚Äôt see, dropped pins I didn‚Äôt realize were missing, and brought structure to chaos. Karren blazed the trails for building up bootstrapping, rethinking the essential order of ideas, and braiding the themes across chapters. I have never seen a pair of people improve a creative project so dramatically.&#13; A crew of perceptive readers made invaluable contributions to the book. Quality-control guru Paul Durbin painstakingly fact-checked every page of this book, delving deep into the studies and stories to ensure the accuracy of all the details. Any errors are mine alone. Queen of signposting Grace Rubenstein clarified key insights, finessed clunky transitions, and pushed me to put the thesis in context. Master idea crafter Reb Rebele led the charge to amplify the big ‚Äúaha!‚Äù and increase conceptual cohesion. Book detective Stacey Kalish hunted down stories and provided meta-scaffolding.
